Dr. M. PEEKY,
M.R.0.5., L.S A , &c.,
SPECIALIST for the Treatment of Nervous
Complaints, Diabetes, Tumors, &c.
Registered by the Medical 80-r lof N.S. IF. Late House Sur-
geon to the Ctreat JSorthern Hospital, Lond'.n
T am aware that I am not strictly following the paths
of professional etiquette by advertising myself as a
Specialist for the t-. eatment of this class of Hisense, but,
unfortunately for the suffering public in Australia, the
apathy shown by Qualified Men in the treatment of
Chronic and Nervous Complaint* has induced men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It,is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
persons The evidence taken at the Medical Enquiry
hold in Sydney (published in the Daily Telegraph, Nov.
6th. 7th and 6th. 1890) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in which patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money, but what is of infinitely
greater importance, their health is injured also. It is
therefore time that Legally Qualified .lien entered the
field to fight these pests of society with their own
weapons, viz., Advertising.
It is utterly impossible for those who are suffering
from there diseases, who are scattered over all parts of
Australasia, to ascertain which Qualified Practitioner
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertises.
I consider therefore, that by advertising I not only
strike a blow at the Quackery which now exists, but I also add
to the usefulness of my profession and in no way lessen my
personal dignity.
My Qualifications I invite the fullest investigation into. For some,considerable time I was House Surgeon to the
Great Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of observing the many phases of the Nervous and
Sexual System, and to become acquainted with the most scientific methods of effecting their cure.
My sole attention is given to the treatment of these diseases, and when a person consults me he is informed at the
commencement whether I can effect his cure or not. The patient is not deluded with false and soecious promises and
if I undertake the case ’
I Absolutely Guarantee a Complete and Permanent Cure.
My great success in the treatment of these diseases enables me' to give this guarantee without fear.

length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
“ The Bulletin ” will return all unavailable MSS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those se7iding MSS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for the preservation or transmission
of such MSS.

SATURDAY, APRIL 4, 1891.
On Various Kinds of Strikes.
Men on strike should be put down by the strong arm of
the law— Extract from speech by Sir Edward Watkin,
British M. P.
When the British railway-strike was in full
blast, Sir Edavard Watkin, M.P., part of
the aggregate wisdom of the British nation,
offered a suggestion which at once aroused
a responsive thrill through all the wide
Avorld of Capital. The necessity for “put-
ting down ” men avlio go on strike is a fact
that is apparent to the soul of Property in
all parts of the globe. In almost every
“civilised” land provision has been made by
Ihav for extinguishing the masses Avhen they
have a difference of opinion with their em-
ployers, and Avhere no special provision
exists, or where the capitalist finds it in-
adequate, the men who administer the laws
eagerly come to the rescue of the law-makers.
In Queensland, for example, the same
Government which prohibits the unionist
shearers carrying arms has allowed the
squatters to make a Avholesale distribution of
weapons among their blackleg employes, and
should the latter provoke a conflict the battle
Avill be one of guns versus road-metal ; and
when the campaign is over, and the dead
are carried aAvay, and the blood is mopped
up, a squatting J.P. Avill make any affidavits
that may be needful, and another squatting
UP. will hold any inquiry that may seem
essential, and the strikers will have been
“ put down ” effectually. The expedient is
a coarse and common one at the best, but
still it will answer its purpose. In Germany,
the other day, the employes in a large
factory sent one of their number to inter-
view the proprietor concerning a rise of
wages, but that energetic capitalist promptly
gave him into custody on a charge of ‘ ‘ extor-
tion,” and the sentence that ensued was six
months’ imprisonment Avith hard labour. The
English law, however, has struck a greater
and grander inspiration even than this.
When the railway strike Avas in progress,
and Property Avas suffering from temporary
panic, the Recorder of Plymouth asserted
the cause of peace and order by deciding
that any man going on strike was liable to
imprisonment on a charge of “intimidating”
his boss ; and the superior courts and the
House of Commons having both endorsed
this reading of the statute, the result is that
everybody who manifests signs of active
dissatisfaction with his employment can be
promptly lodged in gaol as a means of bring-
ing him to a better frame of mind. And in
spite of these lavish powers the British
capitalist, like his brother in Australia, is
not content, and desires further legislation
with a vieAv to “ putting down ” men who go
on strike. It is more than probable that his
desire will be gratified.
* # «■
Yet if the laAv, either in Australia or any
other part of the globe, is willing to “ put
down ” strikes in the largest and Avidest
sense of the term, The Bulletin, regarding
the matter from a purely democratic stand-
point, will give it every support within its
power ; and if it is prepared to “ put down ”
strikers of every kind and variety, The
Bulletin will gladly assist in the good work.
If the men who originate strikes and the
men who go on strike can be suppressed by
fine and imprisonment, then let them be
suppressed by that or any other convenient
means—so long as all descriptions of strikers
go to gaol together. Unfortunately, how-
e\ r er, the world has learned to regard strikes
in one limited sense, and the wide and
general meaning of the term is entirely lost.
The capitalist not only makes the laws, but
he is allowed to make the language of the
nation, or at all eA r ents he alters and trans-
forms the language to suit his own ends. The
worker, avlio helped to appoint the capitalist
as his law-maker, protests against this pro-
ceeding at times, but at the next election he
returns either the same capitalist or another
just like him, and the process goes on as
before. Consequently it folloivs that the
workman who goes on strike is legally
defined as going on strike in the baldest
and most uncompromising manner, while
the capitalist who goes on strike is not
defined at all, and is merely alluded to in the
papers as a “revival in trade ” ora “ sign of
reneAved prosperity.” Yet every time a
statement appears in print to the effect that
“better prices were realised” in some
branch of commerce, somebody must be on
strike, and the matter requires to be looked
into at once by the bilious eye of the laAv.
And so long as the existing statutes against
strikes are in force it is necessary that
cavalry should be called out to overaAve the
individual Avho realised those enhanced
prices, and that big guns should be pointed
at his warehouse, and that the Riot Act
should be read to him ; and if he refuses to
disperse in various directions Avhen sum-
moned to do so he should be charged by
police and troops, and promptly demolished.
* * *
A strike, in almost every case, is simply a
resolution on the part of a man who deals in
a certain commodity to raise the selling price
of his wares. The shearer who wants more
Avages for the same hours, or—what amounts
to the same thing—fewer hours for the same
wages, is in exactly the same position as the
grocer Avho charges an extra penny per lb.
for his tea ; the one man has his labour to
sell, and the other man sells his alleged
Chinese herb, and they both want to get
more money and to give no more goods in
exchange. When the shearer’s employer—
the squatter—demands that he shall sell his
goods at the old rate, he finds his is
in that condition which is technically des-
cribed as being on strike ; and then he pro-
ceeds to hunt up mouldy enactments Avhich
will enable him to have this overbearing
democrat arrested for striking, and the
standing army is telegraphed for, and it is
announced in the papers that the floodgates
of society have broken, and the old land-
marks are destroyed, and the cohesion by
which things are held together has been
obliterated. And when the grocer’s em-
ployer—the same squatter—demands that he
shall sell his goods at the old price, then he
finds that the grocer is also on strike ; but
the term is not applied in his case, neither is
the standing army telegraphed for, and no
troops are stationed in front of the grocer
lest he should break out, and it doesn’t occur
to anybody that the Avorld is fast sliding
down that track which describes an angle of
45 degrees and ends in perdition. From
sheer force of habit the grocer is allowed to
strike without anybody getting in the least
degree excited over the fact; and yet if—
Avhen that dealer in victuals has marked
butter up from Is. 3d. to Is. 9d.— anyone
should insist on his right to buy it at the old
figure, it would be found that the grocer was
as truly on strike as any shearer or artisan
in Australia or elsewhere.
* * *
And in like manner the landlord who
raises his rent is on strike in a violent form
—in fact, his chronic strike is a greater social
upheaval than all the combined strikes of
the combined labourers of the earth since
the Avorld began. He sells the right to
occupy certain land or premises just as the
artizan sells his labour, and his employer is
the tenant just as the artizan’s employer is
the capitalist. When he raises the rent,
Avhich is an event of more frequent occur-
ence than any other event on earth, he
strikes against his employer, and if the latter
proceeded to argue the point instead of
knocking under in abject submission he
would realise the full force and vigour of the
movement very shortly aftenvards. BetAveen
the land-grabber Avho Avants more rent for his
patch of earth, and the labourer who wants
more rent for his muscles, there is no differ-
ence whatever except in name ; and the
difference in name arises solely from the fact
that the land-grabber makes the laws, and
legislates against the other man’s strike,
while his own strike passes unnoticed. And
as he also oaviis the newspapers, it natur-
ally follows that the strike of the man
with 455. a Aveek appears under the heading
of “Brutal Outrage by Unionists,” while the
strike of the person with £5OOO a year is
published as “ Wonderful Progress of the
Country,” And in the same way every in-
dividual who has anything to sell and
demands a higher price for it than before—
every individual from the great importer in
York-street doAvn to the tinker, and including
every variety of human being who deals in
anything, or wants anything—goes on strike
periodically and is on strike most of the
time. How much he is on strike anybody
caji find out by noting when softgoods go
up, and then calling round at the warehouse
of Treasurer M‘Millan, and insisting on
buying these same softgoods at the old rate.
And if, when he can’t get them, he uses the
same language which the squatter uses to
the shearer when the shearer has gone up
and is also unobtainable at the original figure,
he will presently have cause to realise that
there are many different kinds of strikes,
and that the strike of a man with a
big stone-front warehouse has got to
be viewed in a very different light from
that of a man with only a bundle of blankets
and a billy-can. And if, in addition, he
publishes a column of strong language con-
cerning M‘Millan, and states that that good-
humoured and gentle-looking little man is a
coarse and brutal-looking individual with
brows like a gaol door-mat, and that he is
camping in a threatening attitude in York-
street; and alludes to him generally as if ho
were only a shearer camping at Clermont,
he will shortly have further cause to under-
stand that a prosperous citizen who is out on
strike must not be libelled with impunity.
« * *
The favourite answer of the capitalist to
such theories as this is that the two cases
are parallel in theory only. When that
mighty landlord, the gilded Burdekin, goes
on strike for higher wages from his tenant,
he does not demonstrate with banners, or
hold excited meetings, or agitate about his
ivrongs ; and when Treasurer M‘Millan
puts up the wholesale price of his soft-goods,
and thus strikes the whole population of the
country, he neither goes about armed, nor
forms himself into an excited mob on
Circular Quay. In fact, as the nation, with
a docility that is amazing to contemplate,
ahvays gives in, sooner or later, to the tribe
of M‘Millan and Burdekin, it would be
strange if they did. But if Burdekin’s
rents had been steadily declining for forty
years or more, instead of increasing, and had
at last reached a point at which his whole vast
properties only yielded him a bare subsistence
—if, when he went on strike by demanding
a small increase, his income ivas immediately
stopped and he was boycotted by the entire
nation—if, in addition to this, special con-
stables were sworn in to Avatch him and the
artillery were sent down to keep an eye upon
him, and bills were introduced in Parli-
ment for his suppression, and the news-
papers called him a low incendiary Avhenever
a fire took place anyAvhere within ten miles
of him, and alluded to him as a “Brutal
Outrage ” when there Avas a fight in a public-
house that he had never heard of between
tAvo men Avhom he had never seen, and if
people wrote in the correspondence column
stating that they had passed his house
and were surprised to see Avhat a hideous-
looking ruffian he was—then the smiling
and affable Burdekin would be the
most patient individual on earth if he
did nothing Avorse than Avalk through the
street Avith a flag and hold an indignation
meeting. And if M‘Millan’s importing
business were boycotted by the entire popu-
lation because of an extra halfpenny per
yard on the calico, and he Avere left with
nothing but a lot of unsaleable stock and
some bills which he could not provide for,
and in addition the Telegrabh and the S.M.
Herald and the Argus demanded that he
should be charged by cavalry and have guns
fired at him, it is possible that even that
placid capitalist would rile up and dosomething
which he might not do under other circum-
stances. And if, instead of one Burdekin
and one M‘Millan, there Avere eighteen
thousand of them, all suffering from the same
grievance, and all being equally insulted
by the newspapers, the Government, the
churches, and all the other adjuncts
of respectability, and all being harassed
by the police and Avatched by troops
Avho were exhorted to “ fire low ”
when they came along ; and these troubles
arose entirely from the fact that Bur-
dekin had raised the rent of a Aveather-
board cottage, and thus gone on strike
against his tenant —Avell, under such condi-
tions, we would be more anxious to give any
or all of these capitalists a wide berth, than
to steer clear of the most truculent shearer
among all the shearers now on strike in
Queensland.
* * *
The theory that strikes ought to be
abolished, and strikers “put down” by the
strong arm of the Lw, is one that has already
commended itself strongly to the “governing
classes” in Australia, and, on the whole,
The Bulletin is inclined to encourage this
policy, principally out of curiosity to watch
the results of such an experiment. It Avants
to see some statute passed which will put
doAvn the entire gathering of landlords when
they strike for higher rents, and which will
extinguish the importer when he strikes for
more profits, and squash the whole tribe of
employers Avhen they strike for more work
at the same Avages, or the same Avork at less
Avages, and come down heavily on the usurer
Avhen he strikes for more interest, and then,
if a clause is tacked on providing that the
labourer shall also bo suppressed Avhen hr,
wants to go on strike, this paper would sup-
port the measure gladly. But Avhen a Parlia-
ment, composed of men who have been on
various kinds of strikes all their lives, and
supported by a gang of capitalists, employers,
landlords, merchants, bankers, and the like,
whose whole existence is one long strike,
proposes to abolish the only kind of strike
that is not its own particular strike, the
performance is the lowest kind of
hypocrisy. And when the Herald
Argus reproaches the labourers of A f .
because of the violence which is alwav/ 1 a
to break out—but Avhich never doesb r a
when they attempt to raise the price Tn
one article which they have for salo° > •
worth Avhile to reflect Avhat sort of cm 1 18
the placid proprietor of either paper ufiS
indulge in if he were harried by speG 1
constables armed Avith guns, because he lv 1
raised the price of a display “ad.” on tl
inside page ; and with this possibility ?!
view, we come to the conclusion that thin J
might easily be a great deal worse than thL
are—for the capitalist.
Maoriland and Federation.
Tim Maori land Parliament which sent delegates
to the Australian Federal Convention was wise
Had it declined to send them it would perhaps
have been wiser still. The wisdom displayed
was partly shown in the choice of representa-
tives, but chielly in the character of the resolu-
tion which declared that: “ While New Zea-
land sympathises with the aspirations of the
Australian colonies for a Federal Union, such
delegates [the N.Z. ones] are not authorised
to bind this colony to come under any Federal
Constitution.” Oddly enough, both delegates
were men who had held commissions in the
British regular forces. Sir George Grey, the
elder of the two, had not alone been a soldier
but, as a vice-regent in troublous times and
trying circumstances, had secured a reputation
second to none—a reputation which shone the
brighter for the neglect and slights intended to
tarnish it. Yet, not even to such a delegate
with such a name, would the Maoriland Parlia-
ment entrust binding powers a tithe so im-
portant as those entrusted by the Church to the
poor French, Italian, Spanish, and Irish peasant
who, after a veneer of doubtful Latin and more
doubtful theology, is sent into the world as a
priest to loosen the bonds of repentant sinners
and tie with uncompromising tightness the
meshes of mockers. That there is some cogent
reason for New Zealand’s action is obvious.
Let us discover what that reason is.
# # #
It occasionally happens that a representative
body, whose members are not perceptibly
superior to their constituents in mental endow-
ments, is guided by a sort of intuitive common-
sense to see at a glance where the true interest
of the country lies. It was so in England
when, before Cromwell throttled the First
Republic of 1649-53—an omnipotent Cromwell
will be required to strangle the next one—the
peers were voted “ useless and dangerous ” and
the monarch “unnecessary, burthensome and
dangerousin America when, with halters
round their necks, the members at Philadelphia
proclaimed the glad tidings of national inde-
pendence ; and in France when the majority
voted that Louis Seize and his breed had lasted
so long that it was high time to shorten them. It
will be so in the near future when European
assemblies, satisfied that kings and kaisers
should be obliged to work for a living if only to
support their families without appealing to pub-
lic charity, will send properly vised passports to
the various monarchs, with gendarmes to convey
the rulers to the frontiers—on tumbrils, if
necessary. The same sound judgment prompted
even the corrupt so-called Irish Parliament of
90 years ago to look with misgiving on the offer
of a closer union with the big island over the
way, till British gold, to meet the extravagance
of the members and British titles to satisfy the
vanity of their wives, prevailed over pati’iotism
and Ireland became a huge workhouse with
peasants for paupers and bankrupt landlords for
overseers. It is an instinctive fear of the pos-
sible result that induces the Maorilanders to
stand like the fox in the fable at the mouth
of the sick lion’s cave, while wishing the
occupant a speedy recovery, declining to go
inside. As the footsteps all going the one way
prompted the fox to remain bowing at the
entrance in order to avoid complications, so
Maoriland is induced to stand and wait by the
reflection that, whenever a small island allies
itself with a large one, the big partner usually
gets on top and stays there.
* * *
It is always thus when islands, having in
themselves all the physical powers of area,
climate, soil and productions enabling them to
become great nations, are joined to continental
countries whether by force or otherwise. Ex-
cept under favouring circumstances, such as
when the island is so small as to make indepen-
dent sovereignty dangerous or grotesque, the
very wash of the sea is a standing protest
against outside rule. This protest becomes
louder and louder as the superficial extent of
the land and the breadth of the intervening
ocean become greater. There are persons who
affect to believe that there is no difference
between land and water frontiers—that the in-
clusion of Maoriland in a confederacy containing
New South Wales is much the same as that of
Victoria in one including Northern Queens-
land. Such people see no difference between a
Canada governed or controlled from London,
where, even if Canadian representatives were
allowed, they would bo powerless, and a sub-
divided Canada controlled from Washington,
where each State of the present Dominion would
have the same rights as New York or Ohio.
But there is a fundamental distinction, one
whose roots lie deep in human nature itself,
and give every promise of remaining firm this
side of the millennium. The mistake is simii r
in kind to that made by the Imperial Federa-
tionists, who see no reason why all English-
speaking lands should not respond to the action
of political nerves, whose central ganglion was
situated by the Thames. Yet these doctrinaires
would be the first to oppose the proposals to
fix the centre of political gravity in its proper
place on the North American continent, where
the bulk of the people of English speech reside.
In the life of a nation, the day is not so very
distant when what is called the Anglo-Saxon
tongue—probably because no mediaeval English-
man if brought to life would now understand
it—will be spoken by a population of sonic
hundreds of millions between the Gulf of Mexico


and the Arctic circle. To suppose that they
would take either advice or dictation from
London is a supposition worthy of those who
dedicated primroses to Disraeli.
# * *
When giving instructions to its delegates to
hear and see and speak before the Convention
without empowering them to act, the N.Z.
Parliament must have had an eye on the dismal
historical failures resulting from the efforts
made to weld into one administrative system
nations with no conterminous land boundaries
—nations, moreover, whose extent and resources
proclaimed that well-being lay in independence
alone. Take, for example, the case of Britain.
The Romans found her savage but free ; they
left her with a thin veneer of civilisation
and a thick coating of helplessness. No
matter how refined or populous or power-
ful the island became, she would still have
been but a province, and her people provincials,
so long as the strings of government were
arranged and pulled in Rome. When, after
centuries of struggle, the Britons were enslaved
or massacred or driven into the high lands of
Wales or Cornwall, the semi-savage Saxons,
Jutes, Angles, Danes, and other northern cut-
throats were being knit together and started on
the road to progress. Then came the love-son
of the Norman tanner’s daughter with the un-
employed Thugs of Western Europe at his
heels, and down went the English like nine-
pins. Fortunately for the country, however,
the Norman and Angevin princes made London
their head-quarters, so that in time the victim
became the parent State and the victor the pro-
vince. The enthusiasts of the time thought it
possible that the whole of England and the half
of France would be happy together when
governed from London. But Nature had been
beforehand by interposing a narrow strip of sea
not one-sixtieth so wide as that rolling between
Australia and New Zealand. This insignificant
strait proclaimed in a manner not to be misun-
derstood that nothing but force could hold the
two sides in the same political system. Even
had the Normans behaved as mercilessly to the
whole country as they did to Yorkshire and
Durham, and peopled the land with French
adventurers, the descendants of the latter would
have called themselves Englishmen and have
struggled till they obtained a political divorce.
It may be said that it would have been better
for both Briton and Gaul had they obeyed one
Government, whether the latter was on the
Thames or on the Seine. Millions of lives and
billions of money might have been saved by
such a federation. To the philosopher in his
closet such a view may recommend itself. He
may even express regret that men the world
over do not obey the same central administra-
tion, speak the same tongue, profess the same
faith, and resemble each other like rows of
pins turned out by the same machine. In
such a world there would be no room for Alex-
anders and Napoleons, for Cromwells and
Fredericks —perhaps not even for Walpoles,
Chathams and— Parkeses. But the practical
man has to deal with facts as he finds them.
And the fact of all others that presses for
recognition is that, in nearly every case, the
political union of large islands with continents,
or even with islands much larger than them-
selves, is calamitous to the junior partners.
* * *
It may be said that the union between
England and America was a happy one till
Farmer George and his friends forced the other
side to demand a judicial separation. The
colonists certainly were not backward in sing-
ing the virtues of the first two Guelphs and,
for awhile, expressing admiration for the
talents of the third. But it should be borne
in mind that the continent was then the
junior partner and the island the senior.
It would be difficult now, had the connec-
tion held and federation taken place. The
House of Commons would contain about 400
Yankees, including a fair sprinkling of New
York bosses and Western cowboys, while the
Lords would be swamped with pork-men from
Chicago and wooden-nutmeg merchants from
New England. The administration of the navy,
of the army, of India, and of the Colonies would
now be in the hands of men who wore goatees
and spoke through their noses. It would not
he very long before the British began to suspect
that, when an island and a continent went
hunting together, the latter took all the deer
and left the rabbits to their allies. Look at
Ireland again. But a narrow ribbon of water
separates it, not from a continent to be sure,
hut from another island three times as large,
ten times as powerful, and thirty times as
rich. Had Elizabeth and Cromwell done
their work thoroughly instead of half doing
it—had they cleared out the Celts as effec-
tually as the Saxons cleared them out of
Britain or as the English were swept out of
Northumbria by William and had they
dotted the devastated island with British
settlers, the progeny of the latter would long
since have been Irishmen and the island an in-
dependent nation. For had there been no
religious or racial differences political severance
would long since have taken place. The same
might be said of the Japan Islands, had they
been peopled by Chinese and yoked to the
Chinese government system. Directly they
became populous, discovered their own
resources and extent, were permeated by
national traditions and recognised their isola-
tion, there would be an end to rule from Pekin.
# # *
All the arguments that can be used regarding
the ocean’s power to keep nations politioally
apart apply with peculiar force to the proposed
inclusion of New Zealand in the Australian co-
partnership. The former is as large as the
United Kingdom ; it enjoys the varieties of
climate resulting from a latitudinal stretch of
over thirteen degrees in the temperate zone;
its resources are immense, its soil fruitful, its
people one. With the exception of an ade-
quate population—a want which time may be
trusted to remedy—it possesses within itself
all the attributes of a great nation. Political
myopia alone could prompt its people to allow
their country’s destinies to be controlled and its
development dominated by an island nearly
thirty times its size and over a thousand miles
distant. The argument that an alliance is
necessary between England and Ireland, if only
to strengthen both and keep the smaller island
free from foreign influence, has no bearing on
the New Zealand case. The British Islands
belong to the European system —a system in
which militarism is still rampant—and are bur-
dened with the control of hundreds of millions
of subject races. There is no such predatory
future before Maoriland, no probable opening
for twenty-first century Clives or Pitts. She
is isolated, extensive, and supremely dowered,
and will have to solve for herself the problem of
how to keep inside the future Australian
national tariff and outside the Australian
National Federation. To permit any interference
in her affairs by a body far beyond her borders
would be to commit an act of national suicide.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Howl from the Bench.
Justice Richmond, of Maoriland, has sent a
letter of a more than usually grovelling
character to the Federal Convention on the
subject of an Australian Court of Appeal.
The following summary of his arguments
furnishes a forcible example of how a narrow-
minded man can reason when he looks at his
subject with one eye :
Mr. Richmond points out that as British capital is and
will continue largely to be invested in the colonies,-it is
a perfectly reasonable demand on the part of the mother
country that any British subject feeling himself aggrieved
by a decision on his civil rights of a local Court shall, if
the case be of sufficient importance, have his right of
final appeal to an Imperial tribunal. However fair
cojonial judges and juries may have shown themselves, it
is inevitable that persons resident in the United Kingdom,
or in other colonies, who should find themselves worsted
in litigation before a colonial Court, from which there
was no appeal, would in many cases both feel and express
doubt that justice had been done them, and would
be ready to impute the decision against them to local
prejudices and favour. It is to be expected that the pro-
posed measure, if carried, must have a prejudicial effect
on the financial interests of the colonies. The confidence
with which investments of all sorts are now made in Aus-
tralasia by people at home must be largely due to the
knowledge that rights of property will be dealt
wich by the Law Courts on British principles of
justice, and subject to final review by one of the
highest English Courts. It is also pointed out that it
would be, moreover, a loss to both bench and bar in the
colonies if the legal standard to which they are now sub-
mitted were removed, as in great measure it would be by
decisions in Australia being made final. As a matter of
fact, Australian Courts would be degraded by the creation
of a final appeal, as they would sink from the position of
Imperial to mere local tribunals, with corresponding con-
traction of their present jurisdiction.
There are about ten questions that naturally
arise out of this miserable outpouring, and
which Mr. Richmond certainly ought to answer:
(1) If any British subject, simply because the
local Court has not decided in his favour, even
when he is wrong, should have the right of
appeal to the Courts of his own country, is it
not rough that the French or German resident
should not be allowed to appeal to Paris or
Berlin in the same manner, when he feels
aggrieved ? (2) Suppose it is “inevitable ” that
the Englishman ‘ ‘ should feel and express doubt
that justice has been done him ” when an Aus-
tralian Court decides against him, then why in
thunder doesn’t he keep out of Australia ? (3)
Supposing the Australian in like manner should
“ feel and express doubt ” about the jus-
tice of a system which enables an Eng
lishman to drag him away to England in
order to defend his rights on a purely Australian
question—what then? (4) Seeing that the
person who loses a case always ‘ ‘ expresses
doubt ” about the justice of the decision given
against him, why should Judge Richmond or
any other judge excite himself about such a
very common matter, and how does he expect
that, even after appealing to 125 courts, he will
make both parties feel satisfied ? (5) If the
British investor won’t send his money to Aus-
tralia unless he is allowed to carry all his
grievances to an English law-court and drag
the Australian along with him, then why does
he send his beastly money to the United States,
Brazil, La Plata, Chili, Mexico, China, Japan,
Spain, and, in fact, every country on earth,
and trust the local courts of these countries ?
The harmless lunatic who washes-up in an asylum
would hardly have made such an awful exhibi-
tion of himself as Judge Richmond has done
over this argument. (6) Does Judge Richmond
seriously believe that the Australian nation
cares whether the establishment of a Federal
Court of final appeal would be “a loss
to both bench and bar,” or not; and in any case
how could it be a loss to the bench and bar ;
and supposing it were a loss to both of them
isn’t it more important that a whole nation
should be able to get justice without being
ruined in the search for it than that a close cor-
poration of birds of prey should get a few extra
pickings; and why should the people worry
about the losses of the bench and bar any more
than about the losses of the policemen and the
chimney-sweeps? (7.) If the existing Austra-
lian courts would be “degraded” by the
establishment of a Court of Appeal within the
country, are the courts of every other country,
from England right down to Montenegro, de-
graded from the same cause ; and if they are
not then, why aren’t they ; and is there no way
of preventing a court being degraded except by
making it subject to a foreign country; and if
a court is “ degraded ” by being free and inde-
pendent then isn’t a Government degraded
under the same circumstances ; and if
it isn’t, then how isn’t it, and if it is then is
Judge Richmond prepared to say that Wash-
ington, Bruce, William Tell, Bolivar,
Miranda, Palafox, and other individuals who
gained laurels in the cause of independence
were really promoting the degradation of their
respective nations ? (8) Supposing Judge Rich-
mond’s Englishman is sentenced to be hanged
by an Australian court, and should feel
“ aggrieved ” by this decision, and should
“ feel and express doubt ” about the justice of
the arrangement, why should not he also be
allowed to appeal to the Privy Council; and if
he can be hanged by the local court without
driving British capital out of the country why
shouldn’t a much smaller case concerning a few
pounds also be settled without bringing about
financial disaster ; and, moreover, why isn’t the
criminal court “degraded.” (9) Several other
questions are unavoidably held over through
wani of space.
A Wise Departure.
B. R. Wise tells the New (South Wales Free-
trade and Liberal Association that he declines
to stand for the East Sydney vacancy caused by
the death of J. R. Street, because
The questions of paramount importance at the present
time are those which affect the industrial structure or
society, and the true remedy for social discontent is far
beyond the reach of any tariff legislation. In confirma-
tion of this view, the Labour party has deliberately
rejected the policy of Protection and adopted in place ot
it the tax on land values It is, accordingly,
not necessary at the present time to fight the electoral
contest on the fiscal issue.
He goes on further to show that under “ the
fetish, freedom of contract,” so eulogised by the
capitalistic press, the condition of the British
labourer was worse than that of the galley-
slave, and asserts that—
Those who denounce the tyranny of Unionism should
never forget that the most grinding tyranny the world
has ever seen was the tyranny' of industrial anarchy.
To the often-adduced argument that unless
freedom of contract be allowed ‘ ‘ private em-
ployers cannot carry on their own businesses,’
Mr. Wise states that such a statement suggests
to him the question “ Are private employers an
industrial necessity ?” He does not answer his
own question in the negative, but does not
quite disguise his sympathies and cautiously con-
cludes a logical and careful epistle by asserting
that, “holding such views, his candidature would
not be acceptable to a constituency of employers
whose feelings are still embittered by a recollec-
tion of the late strike.” And, admitting that
he is unable to deny that ‘ ‘ the labour party is
more right in its principal contentions than the
capitalist party which is now opposing them,”
yet he thinks it advisable that a candidate should
come forward in the labour interest “ more
identified by position and past life than he has
been with those personally interested in the
demands of labour.” Congratulating Mr. Wise
on the manly and outspoken Radicalism,
nay Socialism, that pervades his letter, and
agreeing with him that the holder of
such opinions would not be a persona
grata to the clubmen of East Sydney, The
Bulletin takes this opportunity of stating that
if he (Mr. Wise) be a true champion of Labour
he is, perhaps, the only one likely to have the
slightest chance of being returned to represent
the swarming masses of East Sydney workmen,
now disfranchised through the Stock Exchange,
the clubs and the personal popularity and
landlord-power of Millionaire Mayor Burdekin.
As a correspondent wisely puts it —If Mr.
Wise, because of his sympathy with the claims
of labour, has reason to fear the opposition of
employers, how much poorer the chances of a
labour-candidate more closely identified with
the workers ? The workers have need of such
representatives as B. R. Wise —men, not
Radicals by birth, whose convictions, formed in
spite of environment, have been arrived at
after serious thought and study, and whose
public declaration is made at the cost of much
self-sacrifice, including a possible loss of friend-
ship and social status.

Jury Reform.
Referee Sampson, bewailing the stupidity and
indifference of the average jury, suggests that
it would he easy to prepare lists containing
only the names of men willing to serve and
capable of serving efficiently. And then he
satirically wonders how it comes about that the
would-be jurymen—more or less “ retired ”
parties who want something to concentrate
their thoughts upon —never get summoned,
while others have to be continually bribing the
policeman not to leave his little notice. If
Sampson lived in Australia he would want to
know why influential fools, important person-
ages whose dullness is a bye-word, should be so
frequently chosen for special juries in cases
where they go into court with their decisions
already cut and dried. It is not so much that
the jury system is faulty as that the wrong
materials, or rotten materials, are too often
used in the working of it. Yet political re-
formers, who have certainly remedied a few
grand old abuses during the last fifty years,
seem afraid to enquire into that Palladium of
British Liberties, which Dickens, when still a
very young man, found to be not quite such a
glorious arrangement in practice as in theory.
The humourist scarcely exaggerated his
sketch of the juryman chemist who had
to sit through the Bardell v. Pickwick case
whilst his mind was forever wandering to the
shop, left in charge of an assistant who might
at any moment serve his best customer with
prussic acid in mistake for a toothache specific,
or something of that sort. True, the immortal
Justice Starling was responsible for that
particular outrage on the Palladium, inasmuch
as the chemist protested against serving his
country in a jury-box, instead of behind the
counter. But the absurdity of asking honest
and intelligent citizens to be wise under im-
possible conditions is as nothing compared with
the crime of leaving the administration of
justice almost entirely in the hands of a police-
man. Tlffi Law, when it hit upon the Pal-
ladium, forgot to take the possibility of human
stupidity into its calculations, and it still goes
on shutting its bleared eyes to the fact that a
person incapable of distinguishing between B
and a bull’s foot is quite eligible to sit as fore-
man of a jury. There are examinations, genuine
or otherwise, to be passed by candidates for
every branch of the public service save the
branch which is supposed to protect the com-
munity against criminal or civil wrong. And
the time seems still afar off when every juryman
will be required to produce his certificate of
common-sense and educational attainments,
granted him, at the age of 21, by a trustworthy
board of examiners. As matters stand now an
important specimen of handwriting may be
handed up for investigation by 12 good men
and true, six of whom have never learned to
scrawl. A knotty point is left to the adjudica-
tion of fellows who could never have passsd
a simple test of reasoning before a Board,
and proceed to give proof of their
mental darkness in a scandalous miscarriage
of “ Justice.” Nor does the cruellest
exhibition of idiocy disqualify these servants
of their country from repeating their little
mistakes. An contraire, seeing that jury selec-
tors work in comparatively narrow grooves, the
dangerous fool of yesterday is apt to be found
popping up again to-morrow in the same old
place. What a farce was that recent agitation
for the removal of Judge Stephen from the
English Bench whilst the jury system remains
in its present state, unprotested against by
public thinkers, and slobbered over by Car-
lyle’s millions of ‘ ‘ mostly ” who don’t think
at all. Why do professional reformers refrain
from operating upon our diseased Palladium?
In the first place, they despair of being able, as
yet, to persuade dull respectability that its
fetish is a fool. In the second place, they don’t
think of themselves as likely to ever stand in
danger of a preposterous verdict.
Merit alone, and not seniority and merit com-
bined, should be the grounds for promotion in
the Civil Service. This declaration is laid
down, not at the instance of the public servants
themselves, or of the general public whom they
serve, but solely on the initiative of the Ballarat
branch of the Australian Natives’ Association.
Now, firstly, such an alteration would open the
door to servile grovelling at the feet of senior
officials who naturally would select the most
fawning, cringing subordinates on hand when
filling vacancies. Secondly, such principles as
set forth are directly antagonistic to morality
and social progress. It is well-known that so
far as intellectual capacity is concerned the
duties of the lowest officials in the service are
quite as difficult, if not more so, than those of
the higher functionaries, and that the higher
the position the easier will be the work.
According to all ideas of rational justice it is
only fair that the younger members of society
shall bear the brunt of life’s indispensable toil,
and that in proportion as man advances inyears
and correspondingly fails in bodily and mental
vigour, so shall his labours be proportionally
lightened, till finally they are dispensed with
in toto. In these progressive times no one wishes
to dissuade the State from setting a good
example to aggressive and ignorant in-
dividualists. The A.N.A. appears to be both
aggressive and ignorant.

The Sydney Mail wants to know why Aus-
tralia hasn’t raised any pompous monument to
Captain Macarthur in recognition of the fact
that he was the “founder” of the squatting
industry in this continent. We should say
that the monument has been left out' for a good
many reasons. The bust and statue industry is
overdone, for one thing, and Macarthur was a
tolerably mean variety of skunk for another.
Moreover, the fact that a man “founded” an
industry isn’t necessarily a thing to gush about.
Macarthur brought the merino to this country
for his own profit, and if he hadn’t done it
about a hundred-thousand other men would, for
the merino is a peaceful and unwarlike animal,
and it doesn’t require any great mental or
physical vigour to bring it into any country.
There is nothing to drivel about in the circum-
stance that Macarthur was the first man who
imported it, for it is always imported into a
new country by the first man who gets a chance
to steal some grass to put it on, and Macarthur.
stole the first grass. The valour of the pro-
ceeding was nothing compared to that of the
man who imported the first consignment of
mustard ; for if his goods had happened to get
into his eye he would have suffered severely,
but nobody ever expects to be blinded by get-
ting a merino in his eye. Perhaps it has never
occurred to the Mail, either, that Australia
owes a good deal to the man who originally in-
troduced victuals into this continent, and that
if anybody deserves a statue he is the in-
dividual. If the nation is bound to make a
greater ass of itself than usual it might also
look up the brilliant genius who rose superior
to all obstacles and brought in the earliest
supply of seed Avheat, for if he hadn’t known
how to bring in wheat, or if he had failed to
keep the bag of grain in subjection, and it had
broken loose on the voyage and jumped over-
board, we might have been living on ’possum to
this very day.
—
Kaiser William of Germany is reported, in
the affecting language of a daily paper, to be
“ following in the footsteps of his father,” the
lamented Kaiser Frederick, and so far as one
man can follow with his ear in the footsteps
which another man made with his throat, this
story seems more or less probable. The
mysterious complaint in the Imperial listening
organ is now alleged to be cancer, and if so,
William’s reign is likely to be exceedingly
short; and even if it is not cancer but some-
entirely different, the fact still remains that the
Kaiser’s ear is going to bring him to grief sooner
or later. And meanwhile, it is a pleasant and
gladdening reflection that should the said ear be
so very bad some day, that William feels that he
must break something to relieve his feelings, lie
can start 2,000,000 towards France or Austria or
Russia, and make things hum by way of solace
and consolation. A monarch with a large army
and a diseased head is about as bad a tiling to
meet as a wild lion with an inflamed tail. Also
he is a darned sight more expensive ; but, then,
he is the one essential principle of monarchy,
and the human race has to put up with him.
The (Sydney Mail the other day published a
joyous little screed pointing out a “ satisfactory
increase ” of £18,355 in the month’s customs
returns at Sydney, and a decrease of £29,274
in the same department at Melbourne. There
is no accounting for Foreign-trade logic, which
is about as disconnected as a racked heretic,
but if “ Freetrade ” is steadily increasing the
customs duties at the rate of £220,000 per
annum, and Protection is reducing the sama
burden at the rate of £350,000, why doesn’t the
Mail tell the truth about its favourite policy
and admit straight out that “ Freetrade ” is
the bigger load of the two ? And, seeing that
the incoherent paper yells about the abolition
of tariffs, where does the “satisfaction” come
in when the country has to pay more in tariffs
than it ever did before ? And when the revenue
goes up, through duties being piled on by a
Government which says there shouldn’t be any
duties, why does the Mail claim the result as a
conclusive proof of the advantages of not having
duties ?

My Dkah Moor.AiiiNDA,—
N.S. W. “Royal ” Agricultural Society’s Show, at Moore
Park, on 20th nil., despite the drizzly weather in the
morning, was very well attended indeed, the crowd being
topped off by Lord and Lady Jersey. It was opened from
a dais (handsomely though heterogeneously arranged by
Wallach Bros.) by the noble Earl, who really ought to
subdue that cerulean neokscarf, and after the usual
Mumbo-Jumbo had been got through a tour of inspection
was made. In the main pavilion the big pumpkin and
the abnormal carrot reigned supreme, alternating with
jams, jellies, and preserved fruits, all decidedly tempting - ,
but whatever is the use of going in for such
things on a “dozen bottle” scale? The bottle-fruits
especially looked equal to anything we get from America,
and yet a ship-captain complained to me the other day
that he went all over Sydney one day recently for locally-
preserved fruit and simply couldn’t find any. Outside
the equestriennes were see-sawing about the ring, and,
with three brilliant exceptions, who would have adorned
any “Row,” a more wriggly lot of horsewomen I never
saw. Of the four-in-hands, I liked Mr. E. Terry’s span of
blacks with the owner for whip, dressed to kill in white
gloves and boutonniere. Mr. Arthur Allen drove his
dog-cart and pair round the ring in fine style, but the
tooler- of a very showy tandem team spoilt the effect by
letting both horses trot.
The official luncheon was served in a lolly-striped
marquee at t. 30. when an assorted throng sat down to
wrestle with fizz and the mortal remains of various
poultry. There were a number of ladies present, for the
first time in the Society’s history. Lady J. having set the
fashion that way, for which she was inaudibly blessed
by many a famished female with her mouth full of turkey.
The tables were gay with strips of crimson velvet and
bouquets of dahlias and ferns, a large wreath of dahlias
ornamenting the vice-regal board. Lady Jersey repro-
duced herself in copper silk and black wafer net, small
hat of drawn-grey gauze trimmed with mauve chiffon and
small white flowers, white fall with black beauty spot,
and carried a magnificent “ committee” bouquet in white
ferns and flowers tied with Jersey colours. Mrs. Burdekin
in a net gown of black surah trimmed with astrachan >
black velvet zouave and sleeves, small black lace and jet
bonnet with touches of pink and neckscarf of primrose
chiffon ; Mrs. Humphrey in grey figured lustre with shot-
silk sleeves, small black hat; Mrs. Dixon in cream delaine
flowered in pink ; Mrs. Alfred Allen in black corded silk
with bonnet of forget-me-nots. A good many Toreador
hats were sported.
Sydney ’Varsity matriculation exams, are safely over
for this year—the matriculatoresses numbering 42. But
I should think the glory would be dear at the price. I
know of one “pass” who used to sit up till 10 p.m. and
rise at 3 a.m. all the time she was cramming ; she refused
meanwhile all social invitations, and dieted herself on
meat-essence and milk ! If to be a successful she-grad.
meant a lamping screw in the future for the retailing of
her stock of feverishly-acquired knowledge, all little hard-
ships like the foregoing might not signify very much ;
but now-a-days the game seems hardly worth the mental
wear and tear. Most highly-educated women appear to
settle down gratefully on £BO per annum, or so-the
wages of a smart barmaid —which is deemed munificent
reward in the teaching profession, and though here and
there one meets with women who attain to decent salaries
as heads of training-schools, &c., the majority of “edu-
cated parrots ” generally find themselves engaged for the
same figure as the young lady who has never gone
through the education-mill, and whose stock-in-trade is
one part of general knowledge to three parts of kplomb.
Apropos to the exam., I am reminded of a Sydney lady
who once worked herself to passing-point, but fell in
love, married ere the fatal date, and gave up conic
sections for connubiality for ten long years. At the end
of that time the domestic virtues evidently palled upon
her, for she took to cramming again, toed the scratch as
a matron and came cut with flying colours. She was then
filled with a wild desire to gain a degree and probably
would have succeeded but that her pet tutor had a roving
eye and hubby didn’t appreciate being locked out of the
study on evenings;

Randwick blossomed like a rose on Cup Day (30th ult )
for the gubernatorial party came out before luncheon

The opening of the “Juvenilities” at Sydney Exhibi-
tion Building at noon on 28th ult. was the usual cut-and-
dried ceremony. The vice-regal people, squired by
Secretary Gosohen, arrived very punctually and were
escorted to the dais by the veteran Exhibitionist Joubert.
But I must rise to ask in an agitated voice what the
Countess—the poetess-meant by her dressing on the
occasion? Imagine an ecru washing-silk, looking as if it
had passed tHrough many boilers, and trimmed with
cream guipure, topped by wide-brimmed straw hat turned
up behind and trimmed in a wet-chicken sort of way with
cream ribbons and feathers ; add to that pale-blue kid
gloves and a badly-put-on white fall, and you will have
a fair picture of the get-up. Fortunately none of the
other women present had anything on to speak of, so the
contrast wasn’t so awful as it might have been. The
feature of the opening was the setting of the vice-regal
verses (“ One People One Destiny ”) to music, which was
sung very clearly and sweetly by 500 school children,
after the National Anathema. The effect was not
artistic. Either the verses didn’t fit the music, or vice-
versa.
As the heroine in Bland Holt’s latest excitement, “The
Golden Ladder,” at Sydney Royal, Kate Bishop wears her
prettiest dress in Act 1, a white silky- gown with touches
of old gold, caught here and there with trails of yellow
roses. Miss Blanche Lewis, as a Malagese, looking well in
picturesque robes of scarlet and yellow silk with chains
of sequins. There was very little dressing in the circle
on the first night, the main feature was Mrs. Bland
Holt in cream and cardinal—she, oddly enough, has only
been in front once before to see hubby act.
There wasn’t room to wink at Her Majesty’s on Good
Friday night, the “undoubted excellence of the pro-
gramme” (as the heavy-weight reporters say) crowding
the theatre to the walls. As is the rule at “sacred”
affairs, the w’omen present were mostly in subdued walk-
ing gear, the few who turned up in turned-down frocks
wisely keeping their wraps on to avoid looking con-
spicuous. The vice-regal box was given up to a posse of

covered with black lace, and bonnet with touches of pink,
and her daughter in cream with flat hat trimmed with
flowers looking about as nice as any of them, which is
rather faint praise when you consider that one of the
party wore a voluminous white skirt, a black mantle of
forgotten shape, and a bonnet like a modified coal-
scuttle, and that another sported crimson silk gloves and
a red rose in her hat! On the stage everyone was decol-
lete. Maggie Moore wore all black ; Saville chose pure
white cap-a-pie, as she always does when she sings the
“Tannhauser,” “Prayer;” and Mrs. Vanderveer Green
(who shuffled on in what looked like a pair of bath
slippers) was in black and copper-colour.
Some fair but afflicted Sydney girl, suffering from the
very feminine calamity of knock-knees, wrote to a pon-
derous local weekly recently to be supplied with a cure.
In response—l am really narrating a fact—she was told to
“ put a pillow between her knees at night and tie her
ankles together with a pocket-handkerchief.” I hope the
girl repudiated the suggestion. Otherwise it is awful
to contemplate what might happen if the house caught
tiro.
The very latest fad is said to bo “anonymous ”,singing,
and considering how ghastly even the most beauteous
soprano looks when she is gasping after upper C, the
novelty ought to be universally adopted. At an anony-
mous concert all the songsters warble behind a screen,
and the music must be far more rapturous that way tha n
when you are forced to gaze at a short, fat woman with
red arms and a frontal tooth out singing with a fatuous
smirk and her head on one side. But unfortunately, lady
singers do not approve of this unseen idea, for to peacock
forth on to the platform, followed by a short-sighted
accompanist tripping over her train, is one of the reasons
why women like to sing in public.
The reason why the Carington-Horsfall marriage was so
long delayed was the serious illness of the bride, which
commenced in November, 1889, just on the eve of her
wedding, and she is only now thoroughly restored to
health. Those “in the know ” declare that the couple arJ
sincerely attached to one another, and that old John
Horsfall, after an acquaintance of several years with
“Brother llupert,” is almost as fond of that good-
humoured youth as of the lady who is now Rupert’s wife.
Speaking of Lady Jersey, whenever His Ex. publicly
orates in her presence her lips keep moving as if
she were dying to have the music all to herself.
Sometimes slie cannot keep silent, hut whispers hei
views into his willing ear, and then you hear hi 8
obedient echo : “ As Lady Jersey desires me to say,” &c.
—showing that in a different stratum of existence the
Noble Earl would have had to nurse the baby longest, you
bet.
Yours affectionately.

be more conscientiously recommended for age, body

Captain Morris, late of an American seal-
poaching schooner, seized by a Russian war-ship,
narrates his Siberian-mine experiences. The
captain and four Americans of his crew —seven
Japanese hands had been killed in the seizure of
the schooner—were condemned to three years in
the mines, to which they were driven, afoot and
bound, 150 miles. Each was obliged to send up
five carloads of coal a day ere he received an
order for rations, and all were to remain below
during the term of punishment. Morris was
chained to a Polish prisoner by a waist-ring. At
the end of three months the Pole died, and for four-
days Morris worked with the corpse attached to
him, so that he could get the double rations—
thoseof the dead man as well as his own. But in
order to do this he was obliged to send up 10 loads
of coals daily. At the end of four days Morris
could stand the corpse no longer, and as he could
not break the waist-ring he cut the body in two
and sent up the halves with the ccal. The only
notice taken of this was the sending down of a
hammer and chisel so that Morris could release
the chains by which he had been bound to the
Pole. At the end of the first year Morris’s daily
task was reduced to three loads. Time passed un-
counted. One day a man descended into the
mine, being the first human being he had seen
since the death of the Po’ish prisoner. He
showed Morris a paper, and motioned to him to
enter the car. Morris refused, as he feared he
was going to be shot, this being the fate of most of
the prisoners who ascended with their loads. The
officer ascended again, but came back with an in-
terpreter, who explained that Morris’s term was
ended, and he was about to be released. Some of
the other Americans died.
SEASONABLE SONGLET.
How doth the gushy, girly grad.,
Who’s tutored once per day,
Look ever beauteous and glad
Though cramming all she may ?
Her tutor had a melting eye,
He taught by turned-down gas,
And yet her parents wonder why
She didn’t get a pass.

“ Schoolmistress ” is the story of thodoat-
ing principal of a lady’s academy (Maggie Moore)
married to an impecunious and idiotic honorable
sponger (Dot Boucicault), who, for sake of his
family’s feelings, keeps their union secret. To
raise the needful for his little extravagances, the
instructress (unknown to the man of family pride)
consents to appear as Queen Honorine in the
comic oratorio composed by Mr. Clitberow, and,
pretending to visit a country parson, leaves the
school, and money to pay incoming bills, in the
hands of her wedded noodle, who immediately
invites a naval pal (Percy Lyndal) to supper. Now
the honorable is in the power of the governess
(Pattie Browne), one of four girls who remain at
school during the holidays. One of their number
(Emma Temple) is married to a pugnacious
young ass (Chichester) without the consent of
her pa (George Anson), an irascible, admiral,
and her ma (Miss Homer), a meek female,
who live just round the corner. In the absence of
the teacher the young couple prepare to elope, but
the girls, intending to give them a supper before
they go, force the idiot in charge to amalgamate
forces and pay “exes ” out of the “house money.”
The visitors arrive from an army and navy dinner,
and Lieut. Lyndal brings Admiral Anson,
(ridiculously made-up a< a cross between Premier
Parkes and the British Lieu) and a small sea-dog,
along, but the latter worthy fails to recognise the
locality on account of the fog, and his daughter
because he lias just returned from a lengthy cruise.
After supper the house is set on fire by the
crackers of the page, brightly played by Master
Quealy; and Queen Honorine arrives in stage
costume through the window just as the guests
leave by the fire-escape. The Admiral dis-
covers everything ; drags everyone to his
own home, where he locks up his daughter,
her husband and the honourable (made up
like a Frenchman and very quietly played
■with a broken-English, “ flannel-mouthed ”
twang by “ Boucy ”) whom he threatens to rope’s
end in the morning. The prisoners are released,
however, by Pattie Browne, and the turtle-doves
win the Admiral’s consent, principally through
the rebellion of his long clown-trodden wife. The
burnt-out Schoolmistress, offered a stage-engage-
ment at £5O a week, publicly renounces school-
teaching and becomes suddenly awake to the
selfishness, stupidity and snobbery of her bandbox
husband and the curtain falls. The play through-
out gives evidence of immaturity, lacks the finish
and sparkle of “Dandy Dick ’ and “ The Magis-
trate,” and is overloaded with allusions that are
not funny in themselves and have no bearing on
the story. The first act badly wants brightening
up and a big show is lost when Maggie Moore
neglects to give a parlour rehearsal to the
composer. Through constantly playing together,
on intelligent lines, a company may become over-
drilled, may play too much in the same key.
We do not intend to infer that this is the case at
the “Cri.,” but their harmony in the present case,
however, so emphasises the breadth of Maggie
Moore’s style that the prim habit of pedagogism
appears to sit very loosely on her. Geo. Anson,
in a very small part, is intensely funny; Pattie
Browne, a little too much in earnest; the Misses
Homer, Temple and Major, with Percy Lyndal and
Clitherow equal to small opportunities, but some
of the parts are badly cast. Master Whyte, who
plays the “middy,” is a clever lad, but ridicu-
lously under age, and some of the schoolgirlism is
too awfully bread and buttery. For the scene of
the last act, a morning-room at the Admiral’s,
Artist Churchward deserves special mention. It
is delicately grounded in light blue, picked out
in ivory and silver panels and bronze ornaments
with built-out archway and doors, and is lavishly
and harmoniously curtained and upholstered.
“ Schoolmistress ” may run for three weeks, and
it is rumoured that “The Colonel,” Burnand’s
skit on the extinct {esthetic craze, is in pr«oara-
tion.

If managers were to play 4 4 God save the
Queen ”at Easter it would draw houses. Right
through the holidays Sydney Royal circle has
been pervaded by wild, weird wooshers from vvay-
back, who laugh after the manner of the midnight
bunyip in his native swamp, in wrong places, and
tramp out and in heavily with cloven breath at
badly-chosen intervals. Mild husbands have also
been observed to pay five shillings for the privilege
of saying 44 goo-goosy ” to heavy and irritable
infants in the passage at the back of the circle,
while 44 ma” leaned over the front railing and
dropped peppermints in the stalls. Thisreminds us,
somehow, that “The Golden Ladder ’ (“ bladder”
would have been quite as appropriate) is a patch-
work play, whose scenes and situations, when good,
have been annexed by Wilson Barrett from various
sources, and then immersed in a literary poultice
of flabby Rule Britanniaism, supplied by George
R. Sims, who, for once, forgets his liver to make
all the characters talk gushingly on every possible
occasion about 44 the ’art.” As an instance of the
slush Sims ladles out, we may mention the words
put in the mouth of Peckaby, the confectioner
(cleverly played by Bland Holt, though somewhat
out of Ids line), who, reverently removing his hat
and looking up among “.the flies,” piously declares
that he has given his daughter the two best names
in the world —Victoria Alexandra. So far as
Sydney audiences are concerned he might
just as appropriately have called her “Squint-
eyed Sal,” for no one moved a finger
and not a few said 14 bosh.” There’s one
incomprehensible circumstance about modern
stage-villainy. Why does 44 the wretch” always
plot to ruin the relatives of the woman he loves?
Albert Norman must be somewhat sick of being
groaned at, Walter Howe of being virtuous. On
this occasion the latter creditably plays a parson

“Bridget O’Brien, Esq.,"’ holds nightly
crowded and fashionable le-re.cs at Sydney
Garrick. The piece is a Hibernian phantasma-
goria from “daybot” to “fine-alley.” “Bridget
O’Brien, Esquire, ’ is the fresh tagged name of our
old acquaintance Widdy O’Brien, nee O’Toole, and
her present mission is to teach her “son-in-law”
the respect due to her daughter. Her “shweet”
consists of a wondrous “flunkey,” whose sole
recommendation is that lie can spake Frinch so
as the widdy can undershtand him, Nora
O’Bree-on still has “on the string ” an inspired
English masher, who forms the only uninteresting
principal in the personnel. The widdy herself is
courted by Major Tiffin, aspring-kneed fire-eater,
and Benjamin Brewster, a broking sharp, and has
the divil’s own joy blarneying them both. A
garden party, given by the “ son-in-la,” is made
the medium for introducing a cosmopolitan pot
pourri into the Irish stew, the result being
neither a pantomine, nor an opera, nor a
burlesque, nor a ballet, nor a drawing-room
entertainment, but a hair of them all—and a
chignon over, of pure Sheridanesque. John F.
Sheridan takes three-parts of the cake; Gracie
Whiteford, as Nora, gets the other fourth ; and
the rest of the company divide the crumbs pro-
portionately between them. A. M. Melville
makes something of the meagre opportunities of
Mrs. Araminta Sparks, the other mother-in-law,
but is not a telling counterfoil to Bridget, Esq.,
whose creative acting and singing are sui generis.
Gracie Whiteford’s vocal efforts are most decidedly
popular. Stella Tudor, as Mrs. Richard Sparks,
yields a fair quota of amusement, and Bert Royle,
Mr. Ditto Ditto, is interesting both when cowed
and drunkenly defiant. Alfred James, as the
Major, and Henry M. Imano, as Brewster, display
considerable merit; the latter sings a baritone
love-song fairly well. Footman Alfonzo Napoleon
(John Tudor) is a gorgeous apparition with a
Seven Dials dialect. The Brothers Bovis do some
agile sand-dancing, but there is overmuch ballet
for anyone but a baldhead. “Bridget O’Brien,
Esquire,” is the,combined lunacy of F. Lyster and
John F. Sheridan. Its incidental music, very
bright, is Conductor Robertson’s; the songs are
by B. Royle, and the scenery, very pretty, is from
the brush of Lessee Wilson. The house of Easter
Monday broke the treasury record, and fairly
astounded Fred. Hiscocks, who at once (it is his
habit when much moved) burst into poetry.
*’ * * *
At Her Majesty’s, the highly-spiced melo-
drama, “My Jack” —a maze of murder,
explosion, shipwreck, Egyptian desert, hereditary
usurpation, birth, mystery, true love, treachery,
shattered faith, high-falutin, and triumphant
virtue, spread over six acts -has been resurrected
from last year’s property-tombs and threatens to
make another “run.” The cast is the same as
formerly except in the case of Giro Panitza, the
Greek assassin and blackmailer, represented by
Frank Harcourt, rice the late Frank Stephenson,
George R'gnold’s Jack Meredith is up to
standard, and we trust that when we have been
staggering for four days over desert ridges
without a drop of water, our lungs will be as
valiant as are his and our memory somewhat
clearer. J. W. Sweeney is a real, live Pat
Doolan, with a fine roll in his gait. Harry
Jewett’s villain >s a hateful success, and he lashes
the gods into howling fury. Harcourt’s
Panitza has too much fluent English for a
Greek, though the accent is well sustained ;
his attitudinising is often bizarre, but his death-
struggles suggest careful study. A. E, Green-
away, as Charles Prescott, the repentant escapee,
is crushed with woe by the author and dies early
in the evening, unmourned by the audience. Mrs.
George Rignold is dramatically impressive as
blind Mrs. Prescott; and Miss Watts-Phillips
holds public sympathy and admiration on account
of her graceful endurances of stage-sufferings.
Madge Seymour is a scrumptious and lively
publicaness. The other parts are suitably allotted
and the infliction is livened up by nautical songs
and dances, and stray (very stray) references to
“Old England’s flag,” which excite, however,
no audibly responsive chords in the breasts of the
audiences. In fact, “My Jack ” owes its success
to its lurid spectacularity of scenery ; the memory
of which is a nightmare of sulphurous sunsets—
of green, smoky, “serrated ” seas, of Libyan caves
and rocky defiles. This marrow-freezing, melo-
dramatic mush -to be continued till further
orders.

The Good Friday Night concert at Her
Majesty’s, taken all round, was a success. Maggie
Moore did not paralyse folks with her “ Ave
Maria,” by Luzzi, nor were Mme. Vanderveer-
Greene’s soli eventful ; but Frances Saville,
despite a slight tendency to be nasal in the prayer
from “Tannhauser,” touched nothing that she did
not adorn. Neither Mr. W. Walshe (his benefit
is on April Bth) nor Mons. Deslouis were in very
good voice, and after the items sung by our
departing prima-donna the events of the evening
were the “God is a Spirit” quartette and the
duet, “Quis est Homo,” by Frances Saville and
Vanderveer-Greene. Mons. Kowalski played
excellently, forsaking his own method once for the
Halle style, and was encored liberally. For the
last recall he played the “ Swanee River,” when a
smile went round among those who deemed such
an item out of place at a “ sacred ” concert. Now,
the “ Swanee River,” endeared by holy and
tender associations, is just as “sacred ” and,
perhaps, as elevating as any melody may be,

Doctor Carver’s “Wild America” skips
from Sydney after Saturday, 4th April, after a
very successful nine weeks’ season. During the
week the horses and bullocks have been given
away_ to ticket-holders, and fresh novelties are
promised for the concluding days. The genial 1
“ Joe ” Brown has gone in advance to arrange for
appearances in Goulburn on Monday and Tuesday,
and Albury later on. The combination opens at
Melbourne Friendly Societies’ Gardens on Satur-
day, 11th April, and, on the close of season there,
returns to Sydney, win nee they ship to ’Frisco,
having gone, since leaving America, completely
round the world and right through the pockets of
every community on their track.

“Moths,” as produced upon the large
stage of Melbourne Royal, is a handsome, com-
forting entertainment—quite a dream of drowsy
garden scenery, gorgeous interiors, graceful
femininity and gilded sin. Artist Brunton has
supplied Ouida’s naughtiness with backgrounds
worthy of that gifted creature’s florid imagination,
and the nobility have here a wider sweep for their
misbehaviour than they enjoyed at Sydney
Garrick. Generally speaking, a sassiety story,
especially one which is mostly told in the drawing-
room, is best adapted to a small theatre, but
seeing that “Moths” deals with princes and
palaces, and suchlike large institutions, it pro-
bably requires as much space as the management
can give it. One has to discuss an intoxicating
quantity of blue-blood in the course of the evening,
for everybody belongs by birth or marriage to the
first families, with the exception of a casual Polish
serf, whose appearance on the programme suggests
a frost-bitten weed thrown into a hothouse full of
exotics. Even Correze, the opera-singer, is a
markee of long descent, with a topnote that he
can trace back to Noah. The general air of
bon ton is infectious, and_ at the end of the
fourth act, a humble critic walks languidly
downstairs, under a sort of impression that
he’s a duke in his own right. This im-
pression lingers around his mind until he
wants to “spout” his coronet and finds it isn’t
there. Then he remembers that he hasn’t an
ancestor who came over with the Conqueror, but
finds consolation in the thought that he neither
maltreats his own wife nor carries on with some-
body else’s —presuming he be the virtuous em-
ploye of a good paper. The fault with “ Moths ”
is perhaps not so much the superfluity of talk as
that it is always being talked by the same lot of
people, forever talking in the same keys. An
aristocratic sameness seems to prevail. Neverthe-
less, the end of the third act is so continuously
strong and wonderfully wicked that many attend-
ances equal to the moderate crowd of Saturday
night might be expected if Fortune were not
frowning on the old house. The cast is much the
same as that seen in Sydney. Miss Olga Nether-
sole plays the heroine again in an interesting,
powerful manner, carrying her head askew as
usual, and repeating Charles Warner’s tricks of
voice. Correze is Cautley as ever, and Charles
Cartwright repeats his badly-tailored concep-
tion of the Russian prince, being especially
artistic in his curt remarks. Sass, too, is
the lord who feels sorry that his relations
with giddy Lady Dolly prevent him from
offering a virgin heart to her daughter, and Hans
Phillips renews his heroic struggle to simulate a
live duke. Also Miss Watt-Tanner continues to
support the dignity of her old position ; ditto
Miss Emma Gwynne, who, by the way, does her
level best to appear youngep than her daughter.
Half-a-dozen violent dabs with the powder-puff
and two streaks of paint might easily disguise the
early bloom of this pretty little lady, and she
must remember that the part requires her to con-
ceal . the ravages of time, sal volatile and
cigarettes, even though she hasn’t any ravages to
fill in with chalk. All the same, Miss Gwynne’s
suggestion of Lady Dolly is the best work she has
done in Melbourne. Her remorseful croak, as she
hurries off after receiving her child’s well-bred
cuss, is worthy of the occasion, and her
inability to stand up straight is just what
one might expect of such an unmistakably
“cronk ” character. The Misses Maud William-
son and Mary Kingsley are new to their parts.
The former looks, sneers, and acts De Sonnaz
with striking effect, whilst Miss Kingsley is just
delightful as Fuchsia Leach, you bet! True, her
’Murrikan accent is intermittent, but she’s real
smart all the time. In fact, Fuchsia’s a daisy.
* * * *
No Easter change was necessary at the
Princess’, where little “Pepita ”and her minute
foster-sister keep up a steady pitter-patter to sub-
stantial business. Leumane’s strange outburst of
funniness comes as the only remarkable revelation
in this revival. His comicality staggers - almost
stuns—a public who never imagined he could have
so much joke in him, and the highest possible
compliment to his performance is a nightly ex-
pression of opinion that Williamson and Garner
have been loading him up with ginger-ale. This
may be mentioned without offence, because Leu-
mane is about as near to a teetotaller as an
operatic tenor can get, and his present high spirits
are distilled from soft tack.
