The new season at Melbourne Opera House
opened with a bang on Saturday night. Two
previous productions of “ Falka ” on the same
boards have not impaired the drawing powers of
an opera which is well made and harmonious,
rather than flimsy and jingling. It was, perhaps,
better liked on Saturday by an audience familiar
with the work than on the original first night,
when Robert Brough, Yerdi, Kelly, Appleby,
Miss Ivanova, and Minna Fischer, put it through.
The music has a tendency to grow upon unprofes-
sional ears, whilst the story, constructed on the
neatest French lines, doesn’t become tiresome.
In fact, folks appeared to swallow ‘ ‘ Falka ” as
though—like Lay Brother Pelican —they wanted
it badly. Certainly the show has never been
more liberally served in the matter of scenic and
leg effects, or orchestra, which may be ex-
pected to lose all roughness in a little while.
This remark applies only to the brass
instruments, and not to the legs, whose
soft, silken appearance is very soothing.
Miss Clara Merivale scores an unpretentious suc-
cess in the name part. Although somewhat small
in voice and style she is a piquante representative
of the convent schoolgirl, and quite a tender little
poem in tights or skirts. Snazelle (a pictorial
reminiscence of his Devilshoof in the old Carl
Rosa times) is sufficiently tremendous as Captain
Boleslas, the knife-girt brigand whose every scowl
is a mortal thrust through the liver, and whose
remorseless ha ! ha ! might send an elephant into
convulsions. For an interpolated song he fetched
a double encore on Saturday. Fanny Liddiard
also makes a nice, brown, bloodthirsty kind of
Miss Boleslas, and the higher civilisation of lovely
woman is prettily illustrated by Aggie Kelton and
other angels. John Forde seems a lot funnier
and more filling as Governor Falbaek than the
governors one meets outside of fiction, and the
unthankful tenor part of Arthur is not worse
than usually done by one Arthur Salvini,
a tuneful amateur with an Irish face
that accounts for his name. There are
mosquitoes to be dropped in all this ointment,
however. Knight Aston overfools the part of
Brother Pelican, which Kelly made so quaint, and
as much may be said against John Gourlay’s per-
formance of the juggins Tancred. It is panto-
mimic and out of the picture. The whitened face
and almost perpendicular eyebrows stamp Tancred
with the clown’s trade-mark and his antics har-
monise with his too ridiculous get-up. Seeing
that Forde once played the part cleverly, and that
Gourlay could make a Ealbach after the fashion of
his potentate in “Paul Jones,” these two actors
might do worse than work a coup d'exchange. This
is a French idiom invented for the occasion.
m * m *
At the Alexandra there was a red-hot
welcome for “All for Gold,” on Saturday, the
older people being especially glad to see Dampier
once again as the faithful soldier who brings two
little girls into their property and sets the soul of
the Wandering Jew at rest. The drama—in
which Mabel Russell and May Renno have suc-
ceeded the Lily and Rose Dampier of yore—will
keep things moving for six nights. Then
“ Belphegor,” another _ revered memory of the
past.
* * * *■
Also another change, that is not likely to
make much practical difference, took place at
Melbourne Bijou on Saturday. “ Forget-Me-
Not,” poor, little faded flower ! dropped fairly to
pieces during last week, and “ Masks and Faces ”
is now what advertisements would call the reign-
ing attraction. Charles Reade’s out-of-date
comedy can scarcely be expected to reign much,
for the inaugural ceremony was a sad and solemn
affair, marked by all the premonitory symptoms
of early dissolution. Nothing, save the simple,
but artistic, mounting, came up to the expecta-
tions of a Bijou audience, so a yard of critical
comment upon the ins-and-outs (mostly outs) of
the entertainment may be condensed into a
pithy observation that it won’t do. Miss
Achurch, in a traditional “ star ” character
like Peg Woffington, challenges comparison
with actresses who were favourites with the
whole body politic. She, not being a favourite of
that species in Melbourne, is unable to throw the
necessary glamour around a show which is dis-
tinctly moderate to the undazzled eye. The
colourless, mechanical Triplet of Charles Char-
rington, and Mrs. Vane of Miss Bronton, are
almost as irritating as Eille Norwood’s funereal
notion of Sir Charles Pomander. Only two or
three of the occasional apparitions, such as George
Leitoh, are really convincing. All the customary
snap has departed from matters at the Bijou since
the Achureh-Charrington Company came along.
And, seeing how strong the opposition is just now,
the Government had better go in for a violent
change of policy on receipt of this friendly
intimation.
* * * *
The Victoria Hall crowd enjoyed a regular
howler of an Easter Saturday, likewise the Gaiety
nearly exploded with bucolic glee. Slade Murray
is about to visit the latter sanctuary for the abso-
lutely last time, and when his brief engagement
has terminated the voice of Good Old Mary Ann
will be heard no more. She has proved herself a
useful, profitable female, and it’s little wonder
that Tummy is very fond of her. The Waxworks
has struck it rich with a pugilistic kangaroo, who
fights his trainer to a finish with full-sized gloves,
and never descends to a barney. He fights to
win, does the Waxworks kangaroo, and if he
didn’t always get the verdict “on points,” he’d
just about'break his mighty heart. An ordinary
human boxer never breaks anything now-a-clays,
except his confiding backers. A People’s Palace,
under parsonic and much elderly maiden-aunt
patr nage, was to be opened at the Exhibition last
Monday, by the Governor in person. The
Governor out of person would be no good for a
task of that description. Other shows as
usual, and Madame Liebe at the Town Hall
occasionally.
*■ * * *
On Saturday (4th) a matinee performance
is to be given at the Bijou for the benefit of a
blind girl who wishes to educate herself in the law
or medical profession or something. The aspira-
tions of this plucky and studious young lady are
naturally attracting a quantity of gushing
sympathy to her case, but when the benefit has
brought in its hundreds some calm, sober
individual ought to step forward and advise the
recipient to stick to her money instead of laying
it out in dead languages and elderly professors.

At Poverty Point.
Avon Saxon, remembered here as the baritone in Amy
Sherwin’s ill-fated company, is appearing as Friar Tuck
in Arthur Sullivan’s “ Ivanhoe.” It was Avon Saxon
who refused to go on one night at Melbourne Opera
House until poor Mr. Amy Sherwin had handed over his
watch and chain as security for salary.
From report of Sydney amateur entertainment: “ Mr.
H. M'lntosh’s well-timed snoring during the rendering of
‘Mrs. Caudle’s Curtain Lecture’by his sister was very
amusing.”
A bush visitor to Melbourne, the other day, bought two
Bijou stall-tickets. On one seat he sat, and on the next
he coiled his dog. Ejected by the police, his defence was
wrathful—" I (picturesquely) paid for two (terra-cotta)
tickets, one fur me an’ one fur Jim. Jim’s alius close ter
me, an’ I hainter goin’ ter be parted from him fur any
(marigold) man. Jim’s a (scarlet) sight better dorg than
any (bally) bloke yere.” Fined £2 for creating a dis-
turbance, and drifted away murmuring—“Fust (cherry)
chance I get at that (mosaic) magistrate, I’ll set Jim on
ter him, an’ I’ll larn Jim to (sanguinary well) sool every
(carmine) cunsterble he (muddy well) meets.”
A Press cablegram states that the young Duke of
Orleans—an enormously wealthy man, by the way—is
now, or has been, acting as valet to Madame Melba, the
now celebrated Australian cantntrice. This statement is
given on the authority of the Pall Mall Gazette, but
the Times has contradicted the rumour and stated that
the Duke is at Tiflis in Asia Minor. The story about the
Duke’s admiration for Madame Melba is now rather old
to the general public, though, if he has been acting as
one of Maclaine's lacqueys, it is plain that she can have
known nothing of it. Many weeks ago a paragraph
appeared in many London papers, including Lloyd’s, to
the effect that the Duke of Orleans was madly in love
with the fair Australian and had followed her from
Brussels to London and thence to St. Petersburg. A
later cable states that the rumour arose from the resem-
blance borne by the valet, who accompanied Melba to the
Paris railway depot when she saw Hubby Armstrong off
to Australia, to Orleans.
What does Mayor Burdekin mean to do with Sydney’s
leviathan organ? He has actually refused the use of it
to an alleged famous French organist, Mens. A. Guil-
mant, for a series of recitals. The argument that these
recitals would seriously affect the success of Mons. Wie-
gand’s engagement is not to be entertained. Sydney’s
organ was not put up, or should not have been put up, as
a matter of speculation, but as a source of public plea-
sure—a refining influence—and the people of Sydney
should, therefore, be allowed every opportunity of hear-
ing the best talent available, if Guilmant be a more
accomplished organist than Wiegand, so much the worse
for Sydney, but the latter’s value will not be increased by
precluding the former from playing. The question seems
to have been studied from the standpoint of the huckster.
Highly creditable is this Bijou Melbourne benefit for
Miss Aston, the brave, blind University student. The
Austral Salon really does something sensible for once.
Madame Simonsen, as previously stated, will have a
special eye to Wagnerian opera. The masterpieces of the
four operas on Nibelungen have never been heard in
Australia, with “Siegfried,” “ Walkyre,” “Tannhauser,”
“Parsifal,” “Der liheingold,” “Tristan and Isolde,”
and all those works, though the plebiscites for the Vic-
torian Orchestra showed this music to he more popular
than any.

assistant-supernumerary star, with little parts and one-
horse songs, at the Bush-street Theatre.

before the Grand Fiddler Edinburgh and his wife, at
Plymouth. “Monti” will be pleased.

in Manchester at the HalG concerts there. Norman
Neruda was out of the bill.

course, at Rotorua. Now, in return, the inhabitants have
christened a local horse-race “ The Burton Stakes.”

A silvery tenor was hauled back from the Brisbane
steamer the other day over an old debt of £2O. He now
lies in Darlinghurst. It would surely have been more
sensible to allow him the opportunity to transmute the
silver of his voice into the gold of the mint than thus to
deprive him of an opportunity to earn his living.

The “ much-travelled ” Smythe hesitated for some time
about taking up Slave-boss Stanley, for fear it should
involve either his being killed or being forced to write a
book. If the worst comes to the worst, he has decided to
choose the former.
The Taylor-Carrington troupe have been playing to
crowded houses in Perth and Fremantle, W.A. Their
most marked successes have been “ Arrah-na-Pogue,”
“ East Lynne,” and “ Strategist.” They are now touring
the country, after which a series of farew'ell entertain-
ments will be given, prior to leaving for Adelaide. This
is the first troupe that has so far made a success in W.A.
The great Finkelstein, piloted by the “ Much-Travelled,”
was a great draw, and the Kate Russell clairvoyant
troupe did fairly well in Perth.
The Metropolitan Liedertaefel’s smoke concert was held

only building available. Among the hits of the evening

the Hues of Daylight Fade ” and “ Going Away,” by the
Society; and Mr. Walshe’s “ Oh, Vision Entrancing.”

The Story of the Grey Kangaroo.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ Did you ever hear about the grey kangaroo on
Mount Sheldrake ? ” said Dick, as we all lounged
about on the verandah after a hard day’s mustering.
“ No, go ahead,” said the boss, “I want some-
thing to send me off comfortably to sleep.”
“ Well, most fellows put it down as a yarn, but
I know it’s true, for I was on the place and saw
the kangaroo often, and had reason to know
it”
“ Here ! make a start, don’t be so long-winded,”
said one of his audience.
“ This grey kangaroo used to be seen on all parts
of Mount Sheldrake run, and all the old hands
firmly believed that it was not a real flesh-
and-blood kangaroo, but an evil spirit. One
ttili'g was certain, some accident always happened
to a man after he had seen the grey kangaroo for
the first time. If you got well out of that you
were right, and might see it ever so many times
afterwards without coming to grief. I got a spill
and broke my collarbone the first time I ever made
its acquaintance, but everybody did not come off
so lightly. Young Jack O’Grady chased it the
first time he saw it, and rode into a wire-fence and
broke his neck ; and the day before Mrs. Camp-
bell, the manager’s wife, died, it hopped right up to
the station and looked in on the verandah. And
every dog on the place tucked its tail between
its legs and hid itself away. I saw that myself;”
here there was a short chorus of grunts.
“It was a great, big beast, grey all over, and
difficult to see until it was right on top of you,
staring at you with its short-sighted eyes. As for
shooting it our best shots tried many a time, but
they always missed, nobody could tell why.
“One evening I was coming home empty-handed
from a long hunt I had had after some lost horses,
when my nag knocked up. There was an old aban-
doned sheep-station, with a hut, and the remains
of a yard about ten miles from the head station,
and I made up my mind to camp there for the
night. It was dark when I got there, and I hob-
bled my horse out and went inside to make a fire.
While I was ‘busy at the job I heard my horse
galloping away in his hobbles, although he was
that done when 1 got off him that I thought every
minute he was going to lie down with me. I ran
to the door, and sure enough you couldn’t see his
heels for smoke-there, coolly nibbling away at
some pigweed where the old yard had been, was the
grey kangaroo. I was so mad I up with a big
stone and let drive at him, and blessed if I didn’t
hit him fair in the ribs; then I ran after my horse,
but just as I was catching him up he broke his
hobbles, and away he went for home, as hard as
he could lick. I gave it up as a bad job and
went back to the hut. By this time it was dark,
and when I entered the door what was my astonish-
ment to see an old white-haired and white-bearded
man sitting by the fire on one of the rough stools.
“Your horse got away ? ” he said as cool as a
cucumber.
“ Yes,” confound him ! ” I replied, “but where
did you spring from ? ”
“ I was just coming up when you started.”
“ Where’s your swag? ” I asked.
“ Why, to tell the truth,” he said, “ I’m in the
same fix as you, my horse cleared out this morn-
ing, and I have been walking all day looking for
him. I was going back to my camp, about a mile
from here, when I saw you in the distance and
came up here.”
I wasn’t sorry to see him, for the sake of com-
pany, and we shared what little grub I had with
me and a pot of tea. Then we lit our pipes and
fell to yarning.
“There’s a queer story connected with this hut,”
said the old man after a bit. “ I suppose you'
never heard of it ?”
• “No,” I said.
“Ah ! it was some time ago, in old Hardcastle’s
time. There were sheep here then. The shepherd
out here was a married man—an old fellow mar-
ried to a young girl. How he came to marry her
is a queer story, too. Seems the old man had
saved up a tidy bit of coin, and he went back to
his own part of Australia meaning to settle down
there. Well, he found an old friend therewith
whom he had been brought up as a boy—regular
chums they had been. This man was a widower
with a daughter as pretty as a picture, and the
old fool fell in love with her straight off. The
girl’s father was awfully hard-up, and couldn’t
pay up on his selection, so the old fellow lent him
the needful, and then a little more, and a little
more, until finally he married the girl and found
himself pretty well broke at the same time. He
returned here where he was sure of a job and
came to live at this hut. I’ve no doubt it
was lovely for the girl, more especially as they
had no children. As time went on the kangaroos
got awful bad, and the Board put a price on their
scalps. A lot of fellows took to kangaroo-shooting
and did well at it. Amongst others there came to
the district a young fellow who hailed from near
the same part as the old shepherd’s wife, and
seemed to know a little about her people.
Of course it was the hackneyed story, he made
his camp only half-a-mile from the sheep-station,
and the lovers met pretty often in the old man’s
absence. The husband, who was fond of his wife,
before very long noticed her changed manner, and
one thing and another caused him to suspect them.
One afternoon he left his sheep and came home.
There was no one in the hut, and no one in sight,
but on the table was a sporting Snider rifle that
he knew belonged to the kangaroo-scalper. There
was a belt of cartridges, too, and the old man
picked it up and put it on quite mechanically ;
then he took the rifle and walked out just as
though he knew what was coming.
You know the ridge just at the back? —well, he
went round the foot of that, andJamet them
coming round the other side, about 50 yards off.
I’ll say that for the young fellow, that as soon
as he saw the old man with the rifle and knew it
was all up, for his arm was round her waist, the
first thing he thought of was to get in front of the
girl and shelter her. And she was just as eager to
protect him from the bullet they both knew they
deserved. So they struggled together, and the
old man he laughed like anything. They had
made a fool of him, and now he was making fools
of them. He was right, wasn’t he !
By-and-bye the young man sings out : “ Why
don’t you fire, you old cur?”
Now, do you know that old fool was just think-
ing whether, after all, the best thing to do was to
blow his own brains out, and let the poor young
things be happy ; but when the young fellow said
that, he couldn’t help himself, but he up with the
rifle and fired, ahd down went the young fellow
with the bullet from his own rifle right through
him.
As for the girl, she knelt by the side of the body
and begged and prayed to him to shoot her, too.
But he wouldn’t. He left her there, and walked
back to the hut, and there everything looked so
lonely and awful that he did what he had been
thinking about doing before. He went and sat on
that log outside there, touched off the trigger of
the rifle with a stick, and blew his brains out.”
“ And what became of the girl ?” I asked.
“ Ah, I don’t know ; she had the sudden deaths
of two men on her soul, so I don’t suppose she was
happy. Now, young man, I’m going to turn in on
these bags. I got a nasty fall in the creek and
came r ight down on a rook and hurt my ribs.
And without more ado the old fellow lay down
on the bags.
“ And has that grey kangaroo that haunts the
station anything to do with this yarn ?” I asked.
“What kangaroo?” said the old man, starting
up quite viciously. “There’s no such thing as a
grey kangaroo on the station ; don’t you believe
rt. ”
“But I’ve seen it,” I persisted.
“Not you,” said the old fellow; “there’s no
such thing—just you go to sleep.” And not
another won! would he say.

I was dog-tired, for I had had a long day, and
slept like a top—it was sunrise before I awoke.
The old man was gone and I saw no sign of him,
but as I started to walk home I saw the grey kan-

yard.”
G. D. Carter, M.L.A. for part of Mel-
bourne, declares there are only six or eight men in
the Victorian Assembly of 95 who do not “ sacri-
fice their pi-inciples ” at the very first temptation.
In other words, the average member, like Wal-
pole’s “ every man,” has his price.
Torrid Brisbane and frigid Christchurch
are agitating for the State establishment of homes
for inebriates—
An East Orange (N.S.W.) alderman refuses
to sign a joint bond for the borough’s overdraft,
because he only owns a “shanty in Summer-street,
part of which belongs to my partner, and some
old clothes.” This dissipates the libel that East
Orange aldermen never possess more than one pair
of pants apiece.
“Allegro” tells a story in the Queens-
lander about a shepherd whose flocks got
“boxed” during a terrible storm. “What did
you do?” asked a man to whom he afterwards
narrated his troubles. “ I just prayed.” “ What
form of prayer did you use ?” “ Couldn’t remem-
ber any set form, so I said—' O Lord, if you’re
still in the miracle line, you’ll never get a better
slant than this.’” Which reminds us of the
brother chestnut about a shepherd who, after a
visit to “the township,” was found one morning
groaning on his knees. His soul outpoured itself
thus—“0 Lord, thou knowest all things; help
me to find that holler log where I planted the
bottle of rum larst night so’s Bucky Joe shouldn’t
find it, for I forget the ezact place, Lord.”
Conditions matter not
(It is a story old),
Seme drink because it’s hot,
And others ’cos it’s cold.
No matter what they blame,
They get there just the same.
Spontaneous Combustion.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I came to Sydney to reform. Matthew Burnett,
the Temperance firebrand, converted me (for the
117th time) last year in Melbourne, but lie having
left for England to recruit his health, “ the boys ”
re-recruited me into Bohemia, and I kept back-
sliding from grace until, fired with high resolve, I
determined to change the venue and mortice
myself in an entirely new—teetotally new, I might
say—set of acquaintances. I succeeded beyond
my wildest hopes.
I presented my Burnett credentials to Rev.
James Wormington, who welcomed me as “ a
bright example of saving grace,” and introduced
me to Secretary Joseph Dubbledoo, of the Anchor
of Safety Lodge of the Noble Order of Go-the-
Back-Way-Ins, who also received me with fervour
and was my sponsor to several fair Sisters of the
Brotherhood.
I speedily became a Shining Light, used to
speak at open-air and hall meetings on the curse
of drink and the blessings of Gospel Temperance,
conducted a Sisters’ Bible Class on Wednesday
evenings, a Brothers’ Experience Meeting on
Friday evenings, and taught a mixed class at
Sunday school every Sabbath morning and after-
noon, besides performing at some allotted mission
work on Sabbath evenings. I also joined the
mission choir, but was rejected because the
harmonium couldn’t keep time with me. In spite
of this melancholy set-back, however, I believe I
would shortly have got a billet as a bank cashier,
but for the unfortunate incident which I am now
about to relate.
I was very popular with our Sisterhood. I was
always reckoned upon as a certainty in any
emergency of unseemly levity, to check at once,
by a grave and ponderous discourse, the rising
worldly spirits of the young and giddy, and I
was particularly happy in impromptu efforts at
frightening “seven-bells ” out of the most hardened
boozers, by vivid descriptions of the torments of
a drunkard’s hell, both in the earthly fantods and
the after-death eternal drought. It was a common
thing for me to harrow a trembling sly-drunkard
into jumping horrors, and bring him to the
penitents’ form in delirious fear. Thus many souls
were more or less saved, and I became an indis-
pensable leader at every function.
The Sisters got up a dramatic entertainment as
a highly moral lesson—“ The Trial of John Barley-
corn.” I was a prominent performer and stage-
manager., It was one grand success, and the
shocking examples that “took the pledge” that
evening were very numerous. Having so much to
do with the stage business, I was excused from
my usual secretarial duties of issuing pledge-cards
and receiving subscriptions,
I was swellishly attired for the occasion in a
long-discarded dress-suit. And, alas ! that piece
of personal vanity was the means of my undoing.
This dress-suit had been at the bottom of an old
portmanteau, and when, earlier in the evening I
had drawn it forth tenderly from its hiding-place,
I saw that it was covered with the tell-tale stains
and grease-spots of old-time Queensland orgies.
I had hardly time to sponge it thoroughly with
benzine and hastily don it, when it was time for
me to repair to the function.
Miss Virtue Sippolyte was a buxom, heavy-eyed
nymph of frigidity, quite 30 wet seasons old.
Nevertheless, she was to my taste —an acquired
taste, I may remark. No breath of scandal had
ever turned a hair of her solidly-set head. And
her ma, a widow, had informed me that the dear,
innocent girl had £3OO a year in her own right. I
had commented on marriage to Miss Virtue, and I
found that her ideas coincided with my own. Her
ma had smiled on our suit, and was once good
enough to prophesy that I would be “a great
power for good if I didn’t backslide.” I had
soothed her qualms on that point, and I intended
on this night to dare rny fate. Now you under
stand why I wore the dress-suit.
It was after the dramatic performance. I had
courageously coaxed Miss Virtue into a back ante-
room, the gas in which had not been lighted. I
will draw a delicate veil over the sacredness of
that dark seance. Love is holy. Suffice to say
that Miss Virtue had referred me to her ma.
I had just imprinted a chaste kiss on Miss
Virtue’s composition lips, when the door opened
and in its streaming light stood Dame Sippolyte
and three Dowager Sisters of the Higher Com-
mittee. They could not see us in the dark,
whereas we could plainly discern them.
Ar ® y° u there, Virtue?” fairly shrieked her
ma. “They said she hentered ’ere,” she said
aside.
“ Ma. Only me and Brother Dipso ”
“In the dark!” yelled the matron, “Won’t
someone light the gas ?”
(( *‘ a minute, dear madam,” I said, hurriedly.
I’m feeling for the matches.”
I was flurried. I drew a lucifer from a vest
pocket and incautiously ignited it in the free old
Queensland style—
there was a burst of flame from hip to knee- —
an ungodly remark from myself, as I suffocated
the rising conflagration—and shrieks from the
women.
I liad forgotten that my pants were benzined !
The ante-room was rushed by Brothers and
Sisters, and they all saw my singed flesh and my
ripped pants.
“You disgraceful villain !” screamed Mrs. Sip-
polyte; “you bold, bad man! Virtue, come to
me.”
Miss Virtue, my affianced bride, gave me one
look of dull, heavy scorn and deserted me in my
distress.
“Brothers and sisters,” said the Rev. James
Wormington (who himself had his eye on the
faded girl with £3OO a year), “mysterious are the
ways of Providence and the paths of sinners arc
full of pitfalls. Hypocrisy is always unveiled by
the hand of Heaven. In the midst of his
wickedness, in the hey-day of his triumphant
dissembling, the deceiver is unmasked. Behold
that wolf in sheep’s-clothing whom we have
called Brother Dipso. The Old Adam was evi-
dently rampant in him all the while he professed
to have put on the new. But Heaven has taken
the scales from our eyes. Heaven ordained that
the spirituous liquors which this confused wretch
imbibed daily—nay, hourly, it may be—oozed out
of him and permeated his perspiration, yt a, even
his very clothing. The simple ignition of a match
has set fire to his alcoholically-drenched garments.
Go, base hypocrite, go forth from the fold and
seek humbly in private if there is forgiveness for
your crimes. Go your ways, ungodly man, and
beware”—here he positively foamed—“beware
spontaneous combustion ! ”
I went.
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
Keep Good Company.
Keep good company or none. Never be idle,
it your hands can’t be usefully employed, attend
to the cultivation of your mind. Always tell the
truth. Make few promises. Live up to your
engagements. Keep your own secrets if you have
any. When you speak to a person, look him in
the face. Good company and conversation are
the very sinews of virtue. Good character is
above all things else. Your character cannot be
essentially injured except by your own acts. If
anyone spea‘k evil of you let your life be so that
none will believe him. Drink no kind of intoxi-
cating liquors. Ever live (misfortune accepted)
within your income. When you retire to bed,
think over what you have been doing during the
day. Make no haste to get rich if you would
prosper. Small and steady gains give compTtncy
with a tranquil mind. Never play at any game
of chance. Avoid temptation, through fear you
may not withstand it. Earn money before you
spend it. Never run in debt unless you see a way
to get out again. Never borrow if you can pos-
sibly avoid ii. Do not marry unless you are able
to support a wife. Don’t overfeed, but look after
your health. Avoid late hours and dissipation,
always beware of the first signs of approaching
sickness. Never neglect a simple headache as it
is a symptom that the liver and stomach are
deranged, and may lead to chronic disease if
neglected. If you find your daily avocati in
trouble to you, that you get flushings of ieat,
nervous depression or neuralgia, you can saj with
truth that your system is run down and til ,t the
only way to overcome such trouble is to ake a
course of Clements Tonic, the only natural flood,
nerve and brain restorative extant. Cl ments
Tonic tones the stomach, aids digestion, in proves
the appetite, forms blood, bone and : nusele,
and ensures regular performance of all lil fls pro-
cesses, thus warding off disease. W. D. 1 Iraham
(senior partner Braharn and Mutch), the crack
tailor and cyclist’s outfitter, 75 King-stre it, Syd-
ney, ran his strength out completely by ( xcessive
attention to business ; his own words describe his
case b-st. He says “Dear Sir,—Kindly send
me two more bottles of Clements Tonic and per-
mit me to express my thanks and gratitude at the
results obtained by taking two large bottles. I
have had a great amount of business worry to con-
tend against, which together with the long hours
I had stuck to it, completely undermined my con-
stitution, and made me feel very tired, weak and
ill, my nerves being quite shattered. I was so
nervous that the accidental slamming of a door
would cause me to start violently, my heart would
beat very rapidly, and I would break out in a pro-
fuse perspiration, afterwards having a sensation
of extreme weakness, lasting for some hours; my
sleep at night was disturbed ; I woke up in the
morning with headache and aching limbs, and
quite unrefreshed. I had no appetite, no strength,
and no inclination to work, and was completely
run out. I was persuaded by your advertisements
to try Clements Tonic, and am a different man
ever since. It gave me new health and strength,
increased my appetite and made me relish my
food ; I increased in flesh, I lost all nervous feel-
ings, and now feel stronger than ever, and am
glad to bear testimony to the value of your
wonderful remedy.”

Unlike Jeff. Davis, he retained no rankle, but

most friendly with his old antagonist, Uncle Billy
Sherman.

Delicious,
When David Proudfoot, the railway-con-
tractor, heard of the sudden death of his friend
“Jimmy” Fletcher, the other day, be said, “I
wish it bad been myself instead of poor Jim
Fletcb er. Now that he is gone, we will hear in
the papers what a good man he was. But he can’t
read it. What a fool of a thing it is we can’t say
something good about a man before he dies.”
Two days later Proudfoot himself died while
under chloroform.
We gather that “Joe” Gy me received
from his uncle, David Syme, between £147,000 and
£200,000 for his share—something like one-
seventh—of Melbourne Age. David Syme is now
sole proprietor, and places his son Herbert in
•Joe’s slices as manager of the Age’s commercial
interests. If this severance of partnership be
based on solid values, the Age may be accounted
worth from £1,000,000 to £1,400,000 as a one-man
property. The daily circulation of the Age
averages over 100,000 copies, probably a few
thousands more than the total circulation of any
four Sydney dailies taken together !
Reginald Murray, the Victorian Govt.
Geologist, once officially inspected an alleged

Dr. Schomburgk, the octogenarian Curator
of the Adelaide Botanical Gardens, who died
suddenly last week in the arms of his son Otto,
was an ideal scientist—a simple-minded, single-
hearted genius. He came to Adelaide more than
a generation ago, with a younger brother, both
being political refugees from Germany. Another
brother, Sir Robert Schomburgk (who discovered
and named the Victoria Regia in the Brazils), was
a fellow-traveller with the immortal Humboldt,
botanist and naturalist, and it was through the
powerful interest of Humboldt that the escape of
the two Schomburgks was connived at. The de-
ceased controlled the Adelaide Gardens for a
quarter of a century, and probably belonged to
more learned societies than any other scientist in
the whole world. To tail off his name with the
initials of all Ins fellowships and honorary mem-
berships would probably fill half-a-column of The
Bulletin. He was as unaffected as a child, and
nobody could be out of ease with him ; but start
him on a hobby and the world’s mealtimes faded
from his ken, though he was hospitality itself.
Rouse him on any question of humanity’s wrongs,
and his eyes would kindle, while his fluent Eng-
lish struggled for utterance and lost itself in a
maze of German gutturals.

One thing is certain about the Great
Northern Shearers’ insurrection : nobody has yet
been hurt.
The late strike will do at least one good
thing—it will give outspoken if not honest politics
to Australia.

years of over two-and-a-halt-millions.

A HARD WORLD.
POLICEMAN: “Hallo! What’s wrong with you?”
INTOXICATED BUSHMAN: “Oh ! it’s very hard to get along in this world.”
Canada is helping England to coerce New-
foundland, not as the daily press says, because
Canada recognises the equity of the French
claims, but because Newfoundland refused to join
the Canadian Federation.
The Australian Fat Men’s Convention,
alias the Federal Conference (the members of
which weigh on an average 14 stone) are being
asked where the Barebones Parliament of the
“ Commonwealth ” is to come in.
A lot of foolish people are now arguing
that Parramatta, the first seat of Government in
Australia, should be the Federal City. One might
as sensibly suggest that all difficulties would be
removed were South Yarra selected as the seat of
Federal Government.

torian constituency as Langridge did on Colling-
wood, for 19 years. O’Shanassy thought he had
the like grip on Kilmore, but was undeceived by
the paltry little Hunt. Kerferd, at the Ovens,
was the next warmest man to Langridge—and
now !

Willis, M.L.A. : “ In America, a man who
is libelled can use the cow-hide and pistol with
impunity, but out here the law will not allow
such things, consequently one has to apply to the
law for remedy.” Yes, but we don’t trust people

The life of President Andrew Jackson—
“ Old Hickory”—gives us a glimpse of the sort
of man Australia wants at this juncture. We say
at this juncture because it becomes increasingly
necessary to smash “ Englishism ” just as Hickory
smashed it, and flatten out all the absurd
Britannic traditions, launching out boldly and
originally, not even Americanly but Australianly,
for our noble,selves.

tion-adopted term, “ Commonwealth, ” implies an
entirely independent Parliament, a Cromwell, and

country. True—and now what does the Herald

The Religious Education League, Mel-
bourne, cannot do better than read a passage in
the new Autobiography of Rev. J. Bickford
(Wesleyan). The writer inserts an extract from
his diary, showing how, while at Bendigo, he had
been disgusted with the sectarian hatred sown by
the denominational schools, and determined to
support a national system of education —which he
sturdily did all through his long Australian
career.
In Queensland many military officers have
been sworn in as Js.P. Lord Justice Mansfield,
who was hardly a Liberal, denounced a Ministerial
attempt to do this kind of thing at the Cordon-
riots epoch. Look at the matter in this way ;in
cases of riot the military act only on the authority
of the civil power. There is, constructively at
least, a balance and checking of powers if the two
are kept thus distinct, but once unite them and
where is the check ?

Senator Hawley, of Connecticut, U.S.A.,
recently tried to scare Congress with the ques-
tion ; “What are you going to do when you find
British war-vessels in the harbour of New York?”
To which the New York Com mercial Advertiser
replied ; —“ We will tell the Senator what we are
going to do in such a dire emergency. With the
first gun fired from a British cruiser we are going
to confiscate every dollar’s worth of confiscable
property owned by Great Britain in the United
States. Englishmen at present own in this
country considerably more than one billion
dollars’ worth of property. We suspect that the

bombardment of an American city.”

Under Oath.
An old lawyer says that many men, whose
common conversation can generally be relied on,
are prone to prevaricate when they are placed
under oath. In common conversation they have
no reason to tell anything but the truth. When
under oath there are generally influences favour-
ing deviation from the line’ of exact accuracy.
These influences are stronger than their con-
scientious regard for truth on its own account, or
their fear of any penalty that might follow ex-
posure, the danger of which is slight. So they
swear to what is not true. Most men, however,
can be depended on to tell the truth, on the
witness-stand or off, in matters which are of
moment to their fellows. The majority of men are
honest; they will not willingly do any great in-
justice to their fellow travellers along life’s high-
way, but, on the contrary, are glad to assist in
making the journey pleasant and profitable. There
are very few men indeed, no matter what cynics
may say, who will put themselves out to work
injury to others ; but hundreds may be found any
day who will go out of their way, cheerfully and
gladly, to say or do something they have reason
to believe will assist or comfort those who are un-
fortunate. It is because of this prevailing senti-
ment in our civilised Christian community that
tens of thousands of voluntary testimonials are
annually received similar to these. W. H. M‘Glew,
proprietor of Vulcan Foundry, Perth, Western
Australia, December 10, 1890, writes :—“ Some
four or five years I was taken with Bright’s
Disease, and my doctor held out no possible hope
of recovery. I was so close to death’s door that
my demise was currently reported in town. I
was afflicted with dropsy, pains in the loins, my
breath came in short gasps, while my urine showed
a large percentage of albumen. When it was
expected that I could not live above a few days or
hours, 1 began the use of Warner’s safe cure. To
my complete and happy surprise, as well as the
amazement of my doctors, the dropsy abated, I
could again breathe freely, and hopes of a recovery
dawned upon me. I have since continued its
use, all my pains have left me, and my strength
has fully returned. I owe my present existence
to its use.” Yuin, West Australia, sth January,
1891 :—“ Some six years ago my health was pros-
trated, and I had to give up work, being then
engaged in mining pursuits in Broken Hill. After
consulting the leading doctors in Adelaide I was
informed that lead poisoning had set in, and also
that I was afflicted with the dread Bright’s disease
of the kidneys. Being urged to try Warner’s
safe cure I did so, and with the use of four bottles
there was a marked improvement ; when I had
taken 16 bottles, I had fully recovered my health
and strength. J. T. O’Donnell, Mining Pro-
spector.” Persons who have been afflicted natur-
ally feel for the afflictions of others ; thus prac-
tically do they attest the sincerity of their sym-
pathy.

Premier Parkes, incensed at the treatment
extended to him by Government House in its social
capacity, lias very properly made up his mind to
stand on his dignity and to attend no functions at
which Governor and Laid y Jersey are presentunless
absolutely compelled by duty to do so. Thus,
whereas in the ordinary course of things he would
no doubt ha e attended the Campbelltown mili-
tary encampment in company with the Earl and
Countess, lie and Lady Parkes paid their visit in
advance and had a march-past all to themselves.
The old man is the official boss of the military
officers, and you bet they were civil to him and
Lady P.
# * *
Apropos to the social disturbance in a high
Sydney circle, it is related that a certain Govern-
ment House young man put it this way within
the sacred precincts of the Warrigal Club : “It
is all very well, don’t you know,” said he, lifting
up lus voice, “ to talk about what you would do if
you were Lord Jersey, but then, donchersee, you
don’t happen to be Lord Jersey. Lord Jersey
has not only to consider the well-known opinions
of Her Majesty the Queen on such subjects, but
he has to consider likewise the status and views
of his wife, Lady Jersey, and also his own posi-
tion as Governor and social head of the—aw—
colony, don’t you know. And then, even suppose
that the considerations I have alluded to never
came into force in the matter, my own social
position would begin to operate, I assure you.”
Which was all carefully carried to Premier Parkes.
* # #
Miss “Concord” Walker, the N.S.W.
millionairess —no sham million, mind you, but a
million and more of solid cash— has given £250
towards sending a contingent of the Tenterfield
Mounted Infantry to England. Miss Walker had
just previously given £lOOO to provide certain
New Guinea “ missionaries ” with a steamer. The
priests and the soldiers will have a long, good
time when the women rule in politics.
» # *
One phase of Chief Secretary Langridge’s
sudden death was not noticed by the Melbourne
press. Some of the responsibility for the recent
hangings rested upon the Chief Secretary, and
three days before his end he had assisted to satisfy
the “Crown” that Wilson—executed on the
Monday—was in sane mind when he cut his
sweetheart’s throat. On the Tuesday morning
Mrs. Langridge gave her husband the newspapers
to read in bed. Almost immediately afterwards
she found him dead, killed, perhaps, by the
shock of the reminder that young Wilson had
protested his irresponsibility on the very scaffold in
the presence of God, the executioner and several
well-known “city gents. ” who had come to see
him strangled. Anyhow, friends of Mr. Lang-
ridge admit that the final decisions in the cases of
Phelan, and Wilson gave him much anxiety, even if
the latter scandal didn’t cost him his life.
Naturally a kind-hearted man, he had endea-
voured to silence his conscience with wretched
legal chestnuts and references to the serious duty
devolving upon a Minister of the Crown, &c., &c.
and failed. Poor old Langridge !
The Parramatta Cottage Homes were,
states the Cumberland Times, “readied up” to
bluff Lady Jersey into undeserved admiration.
Borrowed trees, earthed in big tubs which were
buried out of sight beneath the surface of the
natural soil, gave an Arcadian glamour to a
usually prosaic waste. When the morrow came,
those tubs were dug up again and the trees carted
back to their original owners ! If Lord or Lady
Jersey want to see any public institutions as they
really are, let them pay a surprise visit to any one
of them, and they may count on a surprise recep-
tion. If they will but adopt ihis plan, keep their
eyes open and talk freely in public as to their
experiences, they will not only do N.S. Wales a
service, but set a good example to the world.
# * #
The quarrel between Coroner Youl and
Surgeon Shiels over the body of the executed
Phelan reminds us of the case of Harrison, the
contractor, hanged, with Woods and Carver, the
bank-robbers, for shooting his partner, Marsh, in
front of the Melbourne Public Works Office,
many years ago. The doctors were divided as to
Harrison’s sanity, Professor Halford heading
those who believed him to be insane, while Dr.
M‘Crae led the other side. After execution and
post-mortem, the “ mad ” doctors got hold of the
brains of the dead man for examination. Chief-
Secretary M'Culloch heard of it and despatched
the then Sheriff, Claude Fario, to demand the
remains. When the Sheriff arrived, the doctors
were duly examining the gruesome parcel and
declined to give way. “But,” said Farie, “they
are_ the Government’s brains.” “I can quite
believe that,” said Halford; “they are of a very
poor quality.”

much-discussed breaoh-of-promise case, that Myles
M‘Rae and Miss lima Vaughan were sent away
from the Hotel Metropole, Sydney, for impro-
priety. Now, how does it happen that not a

matter i The alleged occurrence was a crucial
point in the case, and had it been established it

evidence.

An alleged lunatic, interrogated before
Hay (N.S.W.) Police Court, replied to a question
as to his domestic condition—“ I’m sometimes
married and sometimes single, like a good many
more.” No lunacy in that.
* * #
A recent marriage in England has some
interest for N.S. Wales, the bride being the
daughter of Mr. Commissioner Grenfell, who was
shot by a bushranger near Narramine, below
Dubbo, in 1866. Grenfell was on the box-seat of
the coach when stuck up, and was about to draw
his revolver when killed. The murderer was never
discovered, though a well-to-do man in the district
was long under suspicion,
# # *
A kind of religious “strike” just now
prevails in some of the large Melbourne factories.
One of the he-women who spend their time in
distributing tracts, addressing “Mothers’ meet-
ings,” &c., lately spoke somewhat slightingly of
the “ character ”of factory girls; and now when-
ever she visits the factories she is freezingly re-
ceived. A short time ago, in a large clothing
factory, not one out of the 300 girls employed
would accept a tract. An exception, however, is
made in the case of pretty, piquant Mrs. “ Social-
Purity ” Lee, who, up to the present, appears t _
have managed her delicate mission with commend
able tact.
# # #
About the middle of next week another
trusted and conscientious bank official will go off
as usual, leaving the customary shortage behind
him. We publish this statement before he goes,
so as to be ahead of the daily papers. He will be
tall and slim, and have his hair parted in the
middle; also, he will wear a stand-up collar. He
will be found to be an individual who never went
out at night, and it is three chances to one that
he was the sole support of an aged father, also of
a young lady with golden hair and a pale-blue
dressing-gown. Any director having a person
like this in his employ will do well to keep his eye
on him. The usual church member will also be
found out misconducting himself with one of the
girls in the choir about the 15th of next month ;
and towards the same date there will be a big fire
in a Sydney warehouse, but the place will be so
effectually combusted that no one will ever find
out that most of the stock wasn’t there when the
accident happened. Besides, the proprietor will
give a handsome bonus to the fire-insurance official
who values the damage, and thus make matters all
right. A few days later one of the most conspicuous
Christians in N.S.W. will be laid up, owing to a
severe illness, having been badly walloped by an
indignant husband, who came home and found
the good man escaping out of his front window.
This complaint will be called, as usual, inflamma-
tion of the lungs, which will be a lie, also as usual.
Near the end of April there will be serious
rumours about the character of an honoured and
upright citizen, but it will presently be announced
that the vague charges circulated against him
were entirely unfounded, and arose out of that
envenomed malignity which seems to be engraved
on certain base and despicable minds. This is a
short or abbreviated way of saying that he gave
£5O to the father of the girl, and squared the
policeman.

In the big Centennial Hall, Sydney, the
first thing a visitor sees is “John Harris, Mayor”
—during whose glorious reign the place was
almost_ completed—in letters as big as a soap-
advertisement on a cliff. This kind of thing is
perpetuated on the Sydney G.P.O. tower clock-
bell ; in a word, the bell is inscribed with the
“ illoquint ” Postmaster-General O’Connor’s name
in big letters. Another injustice to Ireland!
Daniel’s name should have been placed, not on
the bell, but on the clapper.

raised to any deceased person, that any politician
who happens to take part in its promotion should

* # #
A correspondent of the Field, writing
from Sydney, protests against the utterly reckless
way in which young men are drafted off from
England to Australia to peel potatoes in sixpenny
restaurants and wash up at inferior hotels : “ Here
in Sydney,” he says, “starving in the Domain,
are Vandaleurs, Talbots, De Courcys, Stanleys,
Fitzosbornes, &0., enough to make up a peerage.”
Yes, Australia is the place where the man who is,
a De Courcy, and nothing more, finds his level.

* * #
A well-known Victorian squatter, who
held a great deal of landed property, nominally
transferred much of it to various people years ago
to avoid the full effects of the then prospective
progressive land-tax. One station he put in the
name of a married female relative, who died, and
her husband —he has married again calmly
sticks to the station, now worth £50,000. It is
hopeless for the squatter to try to get it back, fur
as the basis of civil proceedings he would have to
confess that he had taken part in a conspiracy to,
defraud the Government.
* # #
The Sydney public should keep its eye on
the local Gas Company’s attempt to secure from
Parliament electric-lighting concessions. The
proper scheme would be to have the right of
electric-lighting vested in the City Corporation,
which would thus be given a chance of buying
out the Gas Company at something like a reason-
able figure one of these days. To confer further
powers upon the Gas Company will be to further
entrench a noxious private monopoly and enable
it to defy the public. All public services must
in future be controlled by the public.
* # *
Labour-leader Bennett told the Victorian
Charities Commission that one of the grievances
of the Melbourne Hospital nurses was that it was
not uncommon for men who had been handling
dead bodies to be sent straight away, without
opportunity to wash their hands, into the kitchen
to assist in the cooking. Cooking the dead
patients ?

exchange old-fashioned thin rings for new-
fashioned thick rings. English and continental
women are shocked at such an idea.

A TOPICAL BALLAD.
Some folks attempt in reckless way
(Such is their constitution)
To do a lot of things which they
Can’t bring to execution.
Yet latterly the bard has dreamt
(When gin he’d been diluting)
Of folks who nothing much attempt,
But keep on executing.
The Law’s not always put in force
When swindles are committed,
Nor does it seem to “ take its course ”
When bigwigs are acquit-ted.
Though from a rope at certain times
Poor, decent chaps are dan-gled,
To expiate financial crimes
’Tis justice that is stran-gled.
So, let us sing “ God Save the Queen ”
In loud and loyal chorus :
We must eat fat as well as lean—
This fact is e’er before us.
At wealthy rogues we wink our eye,
But O ! we’ll never fal-ter
In doing double duty by
The hei’oes of the hal-ter.
CHURCH BELLS.
( A SABBATH MORNING’S SOLILOQUY.)
Borne upon the morning air
Comes the sound of bells;
’Tis a sound I can’t abear
Church bells make me, I declare.
Angry as a grizzly bear—
Startled in his lonely lair—
When the foe he smells.
Born upon the morning air
Comes the sound of bells;
To the old church, over there,
Buxom wives and maidens fair
Flock to quiz what others wear,
Or, perchance, to smirk and stare
At some spooney swells.
Borne upon the morning air
Comes the sound of bells ;
To the old church, over there,
Goes the merchant, full of care—
Red of face, and white of hair,
Thinking how he can prepare
Borne new, clever, business snare—
Brooding o’er fresh “sells.”
Borne upon the morning air
Comes the sound of bells ;
To the old church, over there,
Crowds are thronging, I’m aware,
All arrayed in raiment rare :
Yet I cannot help but swear
At the very notion bare—
That, among them all, there’s ne’er
One with thoughts intent on prayer—
Parents, beaux, or belles.
E. F.

SUICIDE OR NO SUICIDE.
The New York Life Insurance Company
Pays Its Policy.
It being evident that most cases of self-destruc-
tion involve causes not under the victim’s own
control, and that accident and murder may easily
give rise to a suspicion of voluntary death, the
New York Life Insurance Company, in order to
prevent wrongs to the unfortunate, makes its
policies absolute security. It exercises caution in
granting a policy, and never issues one unless its
officers consider the applicant a good risk. But
when it has once issued its policy and taken the
premium, it feels bound to insure. It does not
insert in its policies the usual clause making them
void in case of “self-destruction, voluntarily or
involuntarily, sane or insane,” and claims no re-
lease from its contract except where there is
evidence of a purpose to defraud. In short, so
far as a life policy may properly be such, the New
York Life’s policies are incontestable from the
moment the first premium is paid. The man with a
New York Life Insurance policy may feelsuro that,
incase of his death, no delay, litigation or loss will
arise from the question of self-destruction—
whether it be accidental or otherwise. If he has
been honestly insured, and honestly conforms to
the simple requirements of the contract, the pay-
ment of his policy is sure on proof of his death.
Chief offices, department of Australasia, corner of
George and Bond streets, Sydney,

on the Sydney market at present.

* * *
Melbourne clergyman (obeying the in-
junction “ Confess your sins one to another,” in
the presence of his late congregation); “It has
been borne in upon my mind within the last 12
months that for a minister of religion to live in
adultery is wrong. I have persuaded the woman
who was the cause of my error to release me from
my vow of silence. She will return to England
and I will never see her again. But if similar
circumstances arose, I would do the same again.
I am truly penitent and anxious to atone for my
fault, and I beseech you to purge me.” Nary a
purge would they purge.
* * *
The last time the Deceased Wife’s Sister
Bill came up in the British House of Commons, a
fat Conservative member made the one brilliant
observation which the party has produced since
the days of Beaconsfield. Breathing heavily, and
gathering himself together for a great effort, he
rose and remarked that “If the hon. member
opposite wanted to marry a deceased wife’s sister
he could have avoided all trouble in the matter by
marrying the deceased wife’s sister in the first
place, instead of marrying the deceased wife,
which was a thing which no religious person could
possibly approve of.”
* # *
A Melbourne youth named Shiell has just
got twelve months for pouring boiling tea over
his mother. If there is one thing more than
another that the average patriot is proud of, it is
the steady increase of the population, and yet
when the increase goes on like this, there isn’t so
very much to be grateful for.

The go-aneacis Hilgrove, N.S.W., used
a Yankeeinstitution —a “bee”—toaid their hospital
on Saturday last. The residents mustered up,
with implements and provender, and started
clearing the stumps and trees out of the hospital
reserve. The district brewer forwarded one cask
of ale to the working picnic, and a deputation is
to wait on him offering to hold “ bees ” every day
in every week, if he can promise that “the cruse
of oil shall not fail.”

Dr. D. T. Edmunds, of Bathurst, a bachelor
medico of hitherto high repute, has been com-
mitted for trial on a charge of indecent assault,
alleged to have been perpetrated on a married
woman. It was the P.M. who altered the charge
of common assault, as laid by the woman’s hus-
band, to indecent assault, stating that he had
no alternative, prosecutrix having deposed to the
alleged facts. The Bulletin, having read the
evidence, has formed a very strong opinion in the
matter.

Christchurch (N.Z.) Weekly Press gives
an elaborate explanation of a literary coincidence
which occurred in December last. A hypnotic
story, “ Who was the Murderer ?” by Cymric,
appeared in the Weekly Press on 18th December.
A narrative, “Who was Guilty?” with an almost
identical hypnotic plot, appeared in the London
Evening News and Post on the 23rd December.
The Bulletin has within the last few months
experienced several remarkable literary coin-
cidences in connection with contributions pur-
porting to come from New Zealand, but has
afterwards generally found underlying them an
easy explanation.

the sin of publication on Good Friday.
* * *
It is noteworthy that no distinctly ugly
woman has yet applied for a divorce under Shiels’
Act. Divorces are exceedingly numerous in Vic-
toria now, and there are seldom less than a score
of applications on the list, but all the female
applicants are more or less good-looking. Gene-
rally, however, the respondent is ugly enough for
two, or even for three. Now and then a dis-
tinctly ugly woman applies for relief under the
provisions of the old Act on a charge of adultery,
and in those cases the respondent is passable, and
his accomplice is a gladsome vision of loveliness ;
but when the case is merely one of desertion, the
ugly woman evidently puts up with it. When
the situation is reversed, however, and the hus-
band is the petitioner, it is generally safe to bet
that the lady is built on such a plan that she
couldn’t show herself outside the house after dark
on Saturday without breaking the Sabbath, and
the co-respondent isn’t much better. Occasionally,
however, there is an exception, and the husband is
found trying to shake off a good-looking wife,

“ Walls have ears.” Theodore Emmond
is charged in New York, says a Dalziel cable-
gram to the London press, with conspiracy and
defamation of character. He boarded with a
Mrs. Dusen and, dissatisfied with the quality of
the food, placed a phonograph in the kitchen and
bribed the cook to set it going while her mistress
was talking. This she did, and then Emmond set
the phonograph talking before the assembled
boarders and Mrs. Dusen:—“Mary, you don’t
want to pay over Bc. a pound for meat anyhow.
It’s good enough for them. You can get it in
Mulberry-street for that. You are giving too big
prices for steak. If they don’t like it let them eat
more vegetables. Yesterday you bought fresh
pie ; how often have I told you to save oc. by
getting it stale and warming it up ? Then this bill
of 25c. a pound for coffee is too much ; coffee at
15c. a pound will do in the future. I have got
enough trouble to make both ends meet without
feeding three men at $5 a week on porter-home
steak.” Mrs. Dusen streaked for her lawyer
without hearing all. Case proceeding.
# * #
The above is one of the thinnest jokes that
ever went the round of the “stupid ’’ papers, British
and Australian. The idea of a phonograph
“ taking anything down ” unknown.to the utterer,
might have been funny When it was first suggested
but it can’t be seriously entertained by people who
have watched, say, Jimmy MacMahon, in the act
of talking, with painful effort and awful delibera-
tion, down a phonograph-tube. Besides, the
scientific toy makes about as much noise as a
watermill when it is working.

Dear Bulletin,—The case of Bourke, the
ex-convict murderer, who at Hamilton Gaol killed
his fellow prisoner, and is to be executed therefor,
reminds me that there are still a few of the old
hands ” left in Victoria. I saw one a year or two
ago in Geelong Hospital, with circular indenta-
tions just above both ankles, caused by the
“irons.” The old fellow was a curious mixture
of religion and profanity. He would sit up in
bed and pray, in his old way, until exhausted;
when he would lie back exclaiming “ I’m damned
[gasp) if I can pray any longer {gasp), God, so I
must [gasp) stop.” Ho died in the hospital after a
short illness. —Yours, F.S.S.

A South Sea Luxury.
ART AND NATURE COMBINED.
It is not on record who invented swimming, or
whether it was ever invented at all. In fact,
nobody seems to have any authentic information
on the subject of how the art was first acquired.
There are, of course, those with poetic ideas on
the matter who could give you all sorts of notions
about it. But we incline to the idea that Adam,
being at all hours for a considerable period in full
bathing costume, took to the water naturally—
being then immortal and consequently undrownable
—and that, subsequently, having tasted the sweets
of laving in the luxurious depths of the Tigris, he
solaced himself for his departed happiness by
continuing the exercise, even when it became
necessary to unfig to so indulge. Then he pro-
bably showed Cain and Abel and his other sons
and daughters how it was done, and so the thing
has gone on from generation to generation, till at
a rough computation there are at the present
time two-thirds of the world’s inhabitants who
can swim. We in Sydney, however, are not
perhaps sufficiently appreciative of the gifts
which Nature has bestowed upon us. Be that as
it may, without a doubt a large proportion of our
city population do not take advantage, or possibly
are not able to take advantage of the natural
facilities offered to them, and do not bathe in the
harbour or the ocean. Assuming that all were
willing and many unable to get to the various
outside resorts, for want of opportunity, the
Sydney Bathing Company stepped into the field
three or four years ago, and established in Pitt-
street their now famous Natatorium, which
enables those who cannot proceed out of the city
to indulge in a dip at any period of the year, with
all or even more than the advantages afforded by
the ocean or harbour itself. In connection with
these extensive baths, Nature has, as it were, been
taken by the nape of the neck and thoroughly
subjugated by means of human ingenuity and a
25-horse power steam-boiler, so that the busy city
man, the pater familias who is engaged all
day, or his wife and family, can be accom-
modated with all the blessings of sea-
bathing and without the trouble of having
to go after it. There, in the very heart
of the city, is ready to hand a stretch of water,
ample for all the purposes of a delightful marine
revel, and with every accommodation, yet minus
the disagreeable surroundings which occasionally
accompany a visit to the public baths. The very
idea makes one feel quite rhapsodical and almost
inclined to quote Wordsworth
There is no poetry about the real thing, however.
It is eminently a practical affair, this Natatorium,
which is now so well known to the bulk of swim-
mers that it needs but little description. For the
benefit of the uninitiated, it may nevertheless be
stated that the Natatorium is situated at the
southern end of Pitt-street, between Goulburn and
Campbell-streets. The baths consist of two
basins, each 100 ft. long by 40ft. broad, and almost
equal in capacity. The No. 1 bath has a depth
ranging from 3ft. Sin. to Oft , and carries 160,000
gallons, while the No. 2 basin ranges in depth
from 3ft. Gin. to Bft Gin., with a capacity of
140,000 gallons. This appears to constitute the
only difference between the two basins. The
water is obtained from near Lady Macquarie’s
Chair by the same pipes, and almost equal
accommodation is provided for bathers. But,
with a view to meeting the requirements of
all classes of the community, a charge
of one shilling for admission is made tc
the No. 1 basin, during the day, the company
there being generally more select, while for the
man with less scruples on the subject of com-
panionship, or perhaps less of the needful, No. 2
is available at the modest fee of sixpence. These
prices apply, however, only to the daytime, as,
alter 6.30 p.m. the admission to each basin is
reduced by one-half. Some curious social calcu-
lations might be worked out from the numbers of
the attendances at the two baths, but that need
not be done here. It is a fact, however, that an
examination made at two or three different periods
of the day has frequently shown that the attend-
ance in tfo. 2, or the second-class bath, was exactly
double that in the other. These figures apply only
to the male patrons of the baths, however, as, so
far, the sterner sex have proved the most regular
attendants. Originally, one bath was set apart
every morning for ladies, but as the patronage was
very" small, this arrangement was altered to three
mornings each week. Still there was no great
improvement in the attendance ; and, finally, after
trying it for two mornings a week, the directors
decided to devote one evening (Monday) alone to
the ladies instead. The new arrangement has
succeeded beyond expectations, and there has
been a remarkable increase, for whereas for-
merly, perhaps, only 16 or 17 ladies put in
an appearance, on Monday last over 160
attended. In fact, the baths are now becoming so
popular with the fair sex that it is under contem-
plation to set apart an additional morning or
evening for them. A great impetus has been
given to the pastime amongst the ladies by the
establishment of the Dual Club, an organisation
which, under a system of strict selection by the
committee of management, permits ladies and
gentlemen to bathe together. In no other way is
it possible in Sydney for a husband to teach his
wife or daughters the natatory art, and so much is
the boon appreciated that already, after a few
weeks of life, the club numbers over 160 members.
Appropriate, costumes are of course insisted upon
for the bathers, and the strictest propriety is
observed, full enquiry be ; ng made as to the posi-
tion and character of every candidate for admis-
sion. By this means the status of the club is
maintained, and it is hoped that great advantage
and pleasure will be derived by the members from
the recreation. In view of the cold weather
which may shortly be expected, it is in-
tended to make arrangements next month
by which the temperature of the water of the
baths will be raised during the winter months to
85°, which will have the effect of making sea-
bathing enjoyable throughout the year. It is also
under contemplation to utilise the powerful boiler
in connection with the establishment to light the
premises by electricity, and to place lamps below
the water. It may, indeed, be said that the
directors are doing everything possible to make
the Natatorium the premier establishment of its
kind in the Southern hemisphere, and it is only
natural to expect that the public patronage will
increase in like ratio. A par ft from the public
swimming baths, there are on the premises about
20 plunge and shower baths, in which may be
obtained hot fresh and salt water, and cold fresh
water. These facilities are largely availed of by
invalids and others, and already many persons
reside for a term at the Grand Natatorium Hotel
(connected witli the baths) for the express purpose
of bathing in the hot sea water, which is said to
have great curative properties for rheumatism.
The baths are under the capable management of
Mr. Barry, who devotes everv energy to the
comfort of his patrons.
Though inland far we be,
Our souls have sight of that imiuoiuU se«
Which brought us hither.

THE REFEREE.
In the Hurdle Race, English rode a most
peculiar race on The Mason, and indeed it looked very
much at one period of the race as if lie were rot wanted,
but the distance told, and at t ie finish his mount was the
only one able to raise a gallop.
# # *
It was a wonderful reversal of form for Strom-
boli to beat Penance so easily in the A. J.O. Sires’ Produce
Stakes. In Melbourne Penance gave him a stone
and a had heating, but on Saturday could not make
him gallop at level weights. When Stromboli won his
llisj race at Warwick Farm, The Bulletin picked him as
the colt of the season.
# * *
Marvel ran a great horse in the Sydney Don-
caster. He made a desperate attempt to catch Paris at
the distance, and gor His nose to the colt’s girths, hut
Bricky Colley- was too great a general to allow him up on
the inside, and Harris had to pull out and come on the
outside. The Marvel crowd saved themselves on Paris,
but again showed that their horse is one of the best
“ milers” in Australia.
* # #
“ Jimmy ” Monaghan was so cock-sure of Paris
winning the Doncaster that he put £lO on the colt, more
money than he ever put on a horse before.
# * *
The finish for the Doncaster was so close that
Bricky Colley, who rode Paris, saved £lO with Lyddiard
on Helene after the horses had passed the winning-post.
* * *
The public showed themselves great judges over
the Doncaster. They backed three horses, who finished
first, second, and third.
# # *
The Selling Race was a very close thing, and
after they passed the post Dan Lawson bet several
“fivers” that his horse Strephon had not won.
# * #
Linnegar rode a bad race on Grey gown in the
St. Leger. After making the pace all the wayand pulling
his mount all over the course, he allowed Ellis to ccmo
up on the inside with Correze,
# * *
Good one as Highborn undoubtedly is, he could
not even make a race of it with Carbine in the Autumn
Stakes. The Champion simply played with him, and
although only cantering at the finish, ran the last six
furlongs in linin'. Ifisecs.
* # *
The Sydney Cup win of W. T. Jones’s thousand-
guinea purchase, Highborn, a five-year-old, with 9st. 31b.
and John Gough in the saddle, was fairly popular, but
the public keep asking where would lie have been had
Carbine remained “ in.” The second of the three-yearer,
-Greygown, under Bst. 51b., was creditable, and it was
hard luck for the owner to get beaten in two big races so
narrowly. The third horse, Yowi (a four-yearer, under
list. 21b.), wasn’t half a length away from the winner.
# * *
Turf morality has not gained much by the
recent freak of the V.A.T.O. stewards in conned ion with
an already notorious mare named Opera. At the allcgi d
request of the owner an enquiry was held into ibis
animal’s monstrously stiff running for the St. Kilda Cup,
at Caulfield. The owner’s request was obviously super-
fluous, inasmuch as nothing but a sudden attack of blind-
ness all round could have excused the powers that were
from recognising the necessity of “doing something” (o
somebody or something. They accordingly made an
example of the jockey-boy by disqualifying him for 12
months. Now, the hoy would cither he pulling under
instructions from ids owner, or trainer, or else lie would
ho working a swindle on his own account, to the
hurt and mischief of his employers. As the stewards
omitted to call upon owner and trainer for any
statement, they must have held them innocent of
complicity in the scandal. Being innocent, the owner
would naturally be an especial object of sympathy to his
friends the law-givers. Wherefore to mark their sense of
the wrong already inflicted upon that unfortunate
man they decided to disqualify Opera far twelve months!
Here we have conclusive proof that the stewards saw
reason to suspect that the mare had pulled the jockey,
in which case the jockey deserved commiseration. And,
as Opera’s enforced idleness will no doubt suit her down
to the ground, it is as clear as a turfite’s conscience that
several human beings will have to suffer so that a degraded
female quadruped may be encouraged to resume her evil
courses at the expiration of twelve months.
# * #
At last one of the Melbourne papers (Age) lias
commented upon Mr. George Watson’s unsatisfactory
starting. Fora long time it has been quite usual to see
short-distance races decided, in a great measure, by the
fall of the flag, one horse getting away ngths in front
of everything, whilst one or two others were fairly and
squarely left at the post. On such occasions a great groan
of amazement is heard from the stand at Flemington, or
Caulfield, but up to now the reporters have not ceased to
slobber over their venerable chestnut, “the Prince of
Starters.” They couldn’t bestow more butter on George
Watson if he were the “renowned caterer” who buys
the drivellers up, body and soul, with complimentary
luncheon-tickets.
John Murtagh Macrossan, the Machiavelli
of North Queensland, the Zalinski gnu of the
Queensland Opposition and a delegate to the Aus-
tralian Federal Convention, died at the Grosvenor
Hotel, Sydney, on Easter Monday, of heart
disease and dropsy. “Jack” Macrossan was an
acrid entity and not a loveable personality. He
was a bitter hater, and his springs of friendship
were too deeply and inscrutably sunk to evoke
spontaneous confidence. He was a Donegal boy,
and was very highly educated indeed after the
Catholic manner, though he was always reticent
as to his youthful training and chances, merely
admitting having attended “local schools.” It
has been openly asserted, and circumstances some-
times lent vraisemblance to the statement, that
Macrossan was originally educated for the
Romish priesthood, which profession he cer-
tainly would have adorned. If he was,
he missed fire, and turned up as a “hatter”
digger on old Bendigo, Ballarat, &c., and
afterwards on Ravenswood, JS.Q. The diggers
there gave “ Jack the Hatter ” his -first rise into
Parliament, and he developed a genius for “con-
tracting,” and was soon reckoned as having an
intermittent periodicity of office as Minister of
Works and Mines. Vapours of scandal frequently
enveloped his private relations to official oppor-
tunities, but he clove through them with un-
daunted front. Lately he was credited with
having been admitted into the ring of “first
robbers ” in the Mount Morgan variations. He
was the political head, too, of the Northern
Separation agitation, though lately he adroitly
’verted to Griffith’s tri-sectional scheme, much to
the disgust of Old Man Billy Coote and other
leaders of the movement. In spite of his abilities
and past j irestige, Jack Macrossan was so distrusted
at the last general elections in Queensland that had
not the new Townsville idol. Bob Philp, tacked
him on to his own coat-tails, his candidature
would have resulted in defeat. Jack Macrossan
was a good sectarian first and a good Queens-
lander only second. But he was above all a lone-
hand player for his personal interests. He was
feared and toadied to, but never loved. He repaid
enforced respect with scorn. His spleen was
venoined, and yet cultured—-a dangerous debater
to offend, fox’, serene in bis own secretiveness, he
trusted no one, and spared no one who trusted
and afterwards failed him. His death is the
removal of a mottled brilliancy. He married a
lady who once dispensed medical comforts to the
Ravenswood diggers, and who never dreamed that
Jack the Hatter, a silent and reserved little hermit,
was “in the running.” He was not quite GO
years old, but physically a broken-down man,
although in his day he had been as good a man of
his inches and weight as ever hammered a drill

This yarn is told of Jack Macrossan, who
died on Easter Monday. A beauteous Hebe had
been imported from Sydney to Ravenswood in the
good time of the Ravenswood rush, and much
additional digger patronage was in consequence
lavished upon the enterprising publican who em-
ployed her. So the usually unwashed digger
would array himself in his “slop "best, and even
put on a “ biled rag,” before doing his evening
“lean-over” at Hebe’s bar. But the gorgeous
civil servant ! Though ids next month’s “screw ”
was already mortgaged “ on the slate,” he revelled
in paper collars, dickies, cuffs, store clothes, and
even low-cut vests ! His “lean-overs ” would be
long and dry. One evening, a pair of these
bush dandies elbowed the counter for an hour,
talking sweet mash to Hebe, but not daring to
order libations, for their slate-margin was full
though their throats were hard. Old “Jack the
Hatter,” grimy and furrowed with toil, sat in
neglige on an up-ended bucket, silent and un-
noticed. After long contemplation he rose,
marched slowly to the counter, prodded his right
thumb downwards on to its surface, and said with
impressive huskiness —“ Less blooming clothes
here, and more rum ! Fill ’em up, Miss.”

man of colour was asked whether he was not the
father of a certain child. “Well,” replied he,
“ perhaps so, to a certain extent.”

A COWBOY’S TOOTHACHE.
FACING A DENTIST FOOLISHLY. HOW SUFFERING
COULD HAVE BEEN AVOIDED.
Col. Metcalf, of Denver, Colo., U.S.A., was
once a cowboy, and tells a good story. “The
winter of 1831,” he says, “ was a hard one, and I
never saw anything like the rain that spring.
When June came we were truly a drowned-out
set. I never was so worn out. I made up my
mind to go to Denver. I hit upon the toothache
for an excuse. My employer was at the home
ranch far away, as I thought; and so having
pressed my excuse on the boys I started. I got to
Denver in the morning. After breakfast I lighted
a cigar and stood on the steps of the hotel, and
standing there came face to face with my em-
ployer. I whipped out my handkerchief, and
whipped it up to my cheek. ‘ Toothache —terrible,’
I murmured. ‘ Toothache, eh !’ he said, looking
at me keenly, ‘come with me and have it out.’ I
hadn’t the courage to say a word, and followed
him meekly round to the office of the dentist. I
sat down in the chair in a' state of utter colapse ;
pointed out a back Loth, and groaned ‘Pull it.’
The dentist had hard work to get that tooth out,
for it was one of the best teeth a man ever had. I
had to pay two dollars for the operation too, and
when the tooth was out, and tbe dentist bad
soothed my wounded jaw, my employer took me
down to the station and saw mo off on the next
train for Kit Carson, bound for the camp. I never
dared to tell any of the boys how I got beaten at
my game.”
To lose a good, sound molar in that sort of
fashion was simply stupid cowardice. Any sort
of quick-witted man would have spoken of St.
Jacobs Oil, which everybody knows to be an
absolute cure for the footbaohe , and for all kinds
or any kind of ache or pain. How easy then, if
ho must fib, to say that he had come to town to
get the great remedy, -and he would have
been believed. Thousands testify to its efficacy,
but one or two instances will suffice to | rove
it. The Hon. Billy Flint, Life Senator, Parlia-
ment, Dominion of Can ala, says :—“ It
acted like a charm in the cure of face neu-
ralgia arising from a defective tooth.” Mr.
Edward Walters, 87 Bourke-street, West Mel-
bourne, writes, “ That members of his family have
used it most effectually for toothache.” Mr. H.
If. Hoermanee. Lawrenceburgh, Ind., U.S.A.,
August 17, 18S8, states that he suffered with
toothache for two days. “I tried,’ he says, “St.
Jacobs Oil, and it cured me immediately, and
there has been no return of pain.” A most re-
markable cute was that of Mr. Samuel B. Shiplor,
Paragon, Ind., July 30, 1888, “I suffered,” he
says, “with pain in my head from sunstroke for
twenty-two years, and was cured by St. Jacobs
Oil; no return of pain in four years!”
