To “Bulletin” Readers.
Readers of The Bulletin, when-
ever situated, will (/really oblige by
informing the Managing Director of
any locality where they may be unable
to procure the paper, and naming
any reliable person who would pro
bably undertake its sale.
Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors
Strictly Original waiter contributed to The Bulletin
will be paid for. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under : —
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. ( Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
2. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
'who ought to be celebrated,
3. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred).
h. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
6. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. Ihe
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not mereb on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Dejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.
Recommended by doctors —Wolfe’s Schnapps.
About this time last year an aristocratic
London fruiterer declared that a peach could not be
obtained in that city for 50 times its weight in gold,
but a few weeks ago three dozen peaches came in
magnificent condition from the Cape and were
sold at about a guinea apiece.
If in need of a stimulant take Wolfe’s Schnapps.

A Street Pull of Ghosts.
Dear Bulletin, —John Basson Humffray,
whose right to mine and to do every other
mundane thing has just been called in by
the Boss Government, was for some time a
resident in Portland-street, Richmond (Vic.).
Portland-street is a cul-de-sac opening into
Rowena Parade, at a spot directly opposite to th®
house formerly tenanted by Peter Lalor, and
which was first occupied by the latter a few
months prior to Humffray’s return to Ballarat to
live. On fine days it is the pleasantest of stroll s
from Rowena Parade to Melbourne, the path
being through Richmond Park and Fitzroy
Gardens, and both Lalor and Humffray would
weather permitting, walk as often to the city as
’bus it or tram it. But they would never
go in company —Lalor has often been seen to
pass down the west side of the street, while
Humffray. walking in the same direction, was
on the other. On at least one occasion the old
Ballarat warrior met Humffray—who, being oniy
a “moral-suasion” man, cleared out of Eureka
Stockade the night before the military attack—at
the turnstile leading into the Park, then called
the Paddock. Neither would speak, neither
give precedence to the other—they simply
glared, each with a hand on the turnstile.
The situation lasted for a moment, and then
Lalor turned from the entrance-gate and got
through the adjacent panel of the two-rail fence.
Humffray was not to be outdone—he would show
Lalor he, too, could be independent of the turn-
stile, so, though he was gouty and bulky, he got
over the panel on the other side. When safely
landed inside the fence, he looked at Lalor and
Lalor looked at him. Both burst out laughing,
but still neither would speak, nor did they speak
for years after.
* * *
Humffray was once so hard down in his
luck that he was reduced to fossicking at Fiery
Creek, now Beaufort (Vic.). It was a local saying
at the Creek that “every pillar carried a nugget,”
the conventional English of the maxim being that
the country was so rich that in every upright of
earth left to sustain the roofs of the drives —it was
too much trouble to timber thoroughly in those
days—nuggetty gold would be found. Humffray
believed in the saying, and tackled a deserted
claim, beginning with the innermost pillar of
a gallery. The maxim was false as to four pillars
into which, at imminent risk to himself, he drove
his pick, for they collapsed and part of the roof
with them without yielding him a speck. Upon
the fifth column, however, a few yards from the
shaft, and his last hope, he made a desperate
onslaught, and he had barely time to pick up a
fifty-pound nugget and get to the shaft before the
whole caved in ? No—it was only a twelve-ounce
lump, and the pillar was standing ten years later.
# # #
Rowena Parade, Richmond (Vic.), men-
tioned above, has, with its extensive Vauoluse, by
the way, had a whole host of more or less notables
living in it at one time or another. Peter Lalor
and J. B. Humffray were two only of the legis-
lators who pitched their tents on its lines. There
was a M.P. for Richmond, who, being pious,
a church-elder, and a secretary of a building
society, set an example which has since been
generally followed, by bolting with the associated
funds to Callao; and there was D. S, Campbell,
who, in addition to many another good service
unostentatiously rendered to his province, deserves
perpetual remembrance as being the first Vic-
torian to plank down a hundred guineas to
the Anti-Transportation League, thereby origi-
nating the “ Hundred Guinea ” Fund, with-
out which the Rev. John West and his
colleagues of the League would have gone bank-
rupt. A certain judge lived at one end at the time
he committed the faux pas of—well, falling hotly
in love (in his old age) with a girl whom he almost
instantly married. The marriage—not the faux pas
—brought down upon him the anathema of Society
and the Church, as Dean Macartney will
remember; and at the other resided a public man
whom the judge, in his young and pre-judicial
days, once defended on a criminal charge—unsuc-
cessfully. The house in which Lalor resided was
built by Mr. Politz, a venerable Hungarian Jew
who had been “out” with Kossuth, and had
enjoyed the dolce-far-niente in a Tyrolese gaol,
with an Austrian for governor. W. IT. Handheld,
erstwhile bowler of “grubbers” and perennial
autocrat of the Education Department, here
erected, ages ago, altars to his Lares and Penates,
and, in the paddock adjoining his cottage, has
often been the recipient of jeering consolations
from a crowd of small boys seated on his fence as
he retired bowled for a “duck” by his young
daughter, who, at the age of nine, could trundle
a better round-arm ball than her dad a grubber.
“ Sammy ” lies, beloved of two generations
of Argus people, and known to all Melbourne as
the queer, dwarfish, but obliging messenger of the
bis: journal, and to a few scholars as a splendid
“Gr/cian,” lived in the street for thirty years,
and one of Sammy’s bosses, F. W. Haddon, who
hulks in the editorial chair, built his first bower
of bliss just inside of Vauoluse. Opposite Sammy’s
house lived a man who had ridden in the first
train from Manchester to Liverpool, and who saw
William Huskisson killed, and who himself did
no inconsiderable work for the public : while, a
few doors lower down, Charles Whitehead, bril-
liant and ill-fated author, who introduced Charles
Dickens to the publishing firm of Chapman and
Hall, lodged for a few weeks not many months
before he was carried from Melbourne Hospital to
an unmarked public grave. There was a curious
tale hanging about the spot of an orgy in which
he and “ Orion ” Horne figured. In this street,
too, starved—and died —the brother of a living
Australian bishop. The man who died was a
mass of erudition, carried loads of learned lumber
in his head, wrote and published a magnificent
book which nobody would buy, couldn’t earn six-
pence to save his life, and was buried by subscrip-
tion, to which his lordship contributed the sum
of one pound. And—in short, the faded, shabby
old Rowena Parade is as full of ghosts as any
street can well be in the young Australias.
P. W.

The Dodo and Queen Bittern.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
When the Dodo first came to Bittern Plains, was
before we whalers knew the mysteries of the pan-
nikin and the use of a double set of nose-bags,
That Dodo is extinct, the last having been
boomeranged three thousand years ago and sent
to Pharoah of Egypt. But another Dodo now
lives, and I shall tell you how she got the name.
Bittern Plains is a ten-mile block cut off from
old Thomson’s run and sold some fifteen years
ago. The purchaser was one Shakerley, a gentle-
man of uncertain and feeble health, but possessing
a better-half whose energy and constitution were
sufficient for both, and who, after life’s fitful fever
■ended for Shakerley, was left a youngish-looking
and robust widow. Her looks, as regards beauty,
are still subject of disputation and blasphemy. A
■change came over the routine at Bittern Plains
when Mrs. S. took full charge. She was childless
■and terribly energetic. In sooth, Mrs. S. is the
best station manager in Riverina. Bittern Plains
is a model homestead. The huts are dressed, the
stables aligned, and the stacks paraded in open
column. Any federated toy soldier will under
stand these expressions. Even the pig-styes are
in direct echelon and why should they not, for
did they not contain Martha and Mary, each of
them twenty pounds’ worth of sow, and doubtless
the squeakers are called Lazarus and Joseph, or,
perhaps, Rahab and Jezebel, Nicodemus and
Agrippa.
Now, this lady—and that she is a lady anyone
who has seen her letters can swear—having drilled
Bittern Plains into shape and made a paying con-
cern out of a swamp, determined to take a well-
earned rest by letting the station for five years.
During this spell her residence would be a free
selection of her own where it is compulsory to do
her time, in the old hands’ lingo. Before calling
for tenders she invited a niece, whom she had
never seen, to stay a week or two with her.
May heaven, if heaven is love, send blessings and
plenty more nieces on the DowageV, for bringing
light and joy and sunshine to a barren district
(barren of beauty and youth).
Here I must tell you that the ordinary wicked
and irreverent traveller had long christened the
old lady “Queen Bittern.” I fancy the sound
reminded them of her strident voice and merci-
less abuse, for Mrs. Shakerley lias a temper and a
will. Simultaneously with the arrival of the
niece, Miss Tigerly, came an interviewer for Mrs.
S. He wanted to look at the run and make an
offer. Out came old Blinker in the one-horse
shay, and the run was inspected, approved, and
let on a five years’ lease to Mr. Lonehand.
There is no doubt that . Queen Bittern set her
cap at Lonehand. It would not have been a good
match, for a man may not marry his grandmother.
Just then it occurred to the old young lady and
the new lessee that Miss Tigerly was confoundedly
good-looking. I, myself, shared and do share
their opinion. I had often seen her before. She
won the skating prize at Dubamble, and rode the
winner of the ladies’ hacks at the Barwon
Blastural and Horridcultural Show. And so-and-
so. It was love.
The Queen tried hard to keep the young ones
apart but failed. So she determined to pack
off her niece before the delivery of the
station. She called the whole proceedings dis-
graceful, and hinted that Miss Tigerly was a
designing minx, and that old Mr. Lonehand
would accuse her, Mrs. S., of match-making.
Poor old dowager, you can’t dam the torrent of
love—you can only damn it.
And now Miss Tigerly has become Mrs. Lone-
hand, and the “whalers” call her the Queen of
the Plains, and Joe the Marine has proclaimed
the late Majesty extinct and named her the Dodo.
The Dodo is in great grief—she loves pigs. The
Queen hates them. Twice has Mr. Lonehand
impounded Martha and Mary and threatened to
shoot them. Mrs. Lonehand does not forget her
aunt’s attempt to stop her marriage. She makes
her time her calls at fashionable hours and speaks
of her as a selector on the run, and charges ten
shillings for a sheep, and has blocked the short-cut
to the selection.
Memorandum : The older one gets the more
aged one becomes.
SCOTTY THE WRINKLER.
Buy Wolfe’s Schnapps by the bottle.
How to train up a mixed family of eleven
in morality and a two-roomed cottage is the pro
blem which worries several Victorian railway
gatekeepers. They want more rooms—and less
family.
J. T. Cole, president-elect of Jamberoo
School of Arts, Kiama, during his opening address
to that institution the other day: u It seems t(
me that intellectual revival is imperative ii
Jamberoo is to maintain its high reputation for
intelligence and culture ” ! ! !
O, wealth of words which promise deeds !
Australia thrills with hope anew ;
Though all else fail our darkest needs.
We can rely on Jamberoo !
Our friend in staid sobriety,
In mellowness we lean on you ;
Should horrors come, we still rely
In jim-jams, on Jim jamberoo !
Our hearts can hold no doubting mist
Anent Kiarna Ghost and you ;

An Aesthetic Handicap.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Diana, silvern, bathed
Rare flowers in tender sheen
The faintest zephyr swathed
The day-blooms folded e’en.
The tiny streamlet dashed
In wreaths and foamy churns ;
The silv’ry fountain splashed
O’er lilies and o’er ferns.
Deep in a fragrant bower
Two youthful souls drank in
The glories of the hour,
The glories of the scene.
All hid in his left hand
Her right confiding lay ;
Each heart did understand
The other, rich as May.
Long held they silent gaze
On that entrancing vi w ;
Nor vainly offered praise
Of empty words thereto.
Long was the raptured eye
Fixed on the beauteous show ;
At length, with heavy sigh,
’Twas thus she murmured low :
“ Beloved, my hand unclasp !
Though ! tis L r ever thine,
Yet now forego thy grasp
Awhile, thou youth divine —
“ Nay, nay, that frown forbear,
And scorn me not, adored ;
But my distresses share,
My own, my bosom’s lord.
“ Though an imperial moon
Begems a verdure bright;
Though Nature seems to swoon
In floods of perfumed light;
“ Though pours the nightingale
His passion to the rose,
I want —ah, piteous tale !
I want to blow my nose.”
JAMES OSBORNE.
Murrumburrah.
A man convicted of a small theft is about to
be sentenced. He looks very slid and hopeless,
and the court is much moved by his contrite
appearance. “Have you ever been sentenced to
imprisonment?” asks the judge. “Never, never!”
exclaims the prisoner, bursting into teais. “Don't
cry, don't cry,” says the judge, consolingly; “you're
going to be, now !”
The depth of grovel to which English
journals sink is incredible, as, for example, in the
last number of the World, where Yates solemnly
contradicts a rumour, by another “ society ”
organ, that Queen Victoria is taking her own bed
with her to Florence, for, says Yates, he has the
best authority for stating that the Queen left at
Florence the bed she took there in 1888 ! Was he
under it, or what ?
Miss Jessie Playfair, who, her father being
a widower, officiated as Mayoress of Sydney
during his term of office, was recently married
at St. John’s, Darlinghurst, to Mr. Frank B.
Walker, son of Mr. P. B. Walker, of Sydney.
The bride wore cream foulard with satin stripe,
and trimmed with Maltese lace shade. She
carried a beautiful bouquet of white flowers.
There were two bridesmaids, and the honeymoon
is being spent at Maitland.

had a gentle spill from a trap whilst driving
through a place called “Dirty Hollows,” the other
day
During the recent severe frosts in England
the driver of the night mail-cart from Canterbury
to Dover is reported to have frozen to death on his
seat. His horse made the round as usual, how-
ever, and drew up at the usual stopping places,
where the officials talked affably to the corpse,
and were surprised that his remains did not
respond. At the end of the journey the deceased
handed over the letters as usual, and had a drink,
and collected his salary, before anybody had time
to discover that he was dead. This last item is
not in the bill, but we put it in ourselves to give
an air < f verisimilitude to an otherwise bald and
unconvincing narrative.

Sergeant Dillon, the newspaper-made hero
of Clermont, has been promoted as sub-inspector.
The Mount Morgan Municipal Council is
an unpopular motley. Not one of its present
aldermen has been elected by the ratepayers, but
all are Brisbane nominees. Its complexion is
polychromatic, varying from the lily-white of the
aristocratic professional, through the deepening
browns of the more or less sunburnt cOsmooolitaris,
up to the charcoal of the blackamoor. A requi-
sitionist for the last nominee put the ease cheer-
fully thus : “We can’t get Archer, the Bellman,
to stand, because he’s a Corporation employe, but
we’ll have a go at the nigger and make it a pie-
bald Council.” Chinaman Ah Boo was requested
to permit himself to be nominated, but his answer
was : “ No tankee, no savee Aldeemanee Ah Boo;
my lespectee allee same.”
0. C. Kingston, M.L.A. and ex-Attorney-
General of South Australia, is Australian-born,
and one of the sons of the late Sir G. S. Kingston,
who was, before the days of “ Gentleman ”
Ross, Speaker of the S.A. Legislative Assembly
and G.O.M. of that colony. The Kingston
Brothers have frequently been cited by finical
“ sassiety ” as peculiar, and the vagaries of one
of them, co’loquially known as “Patsy,” though
that is not his name, were always greedily
seized by cold-blooded Respectability as the
occasion for scorn and cruel merriment, though
his misfortune was due to a physical accident
operating severely on an excitable temperament.
“ Charley,” the subject of this par., has displayed
resolute manliness in every position which he
has been called upon to till, though his
simplicity of personal ideal has been con-
sidered a fair butt for the shoddyocracy As
a footballer, this six-footer (or thereabouts,
though he looks small alongside the enormous
Playford, with whom he has been perambulating
Sydney blocks lately) was a perfect terror. As a
lawyer, he was clever. As a volunteer citizen-
soldier, he was unique. He exhibited his own
strong disgust at the abominations of military
caste by refusing to be “commissioned” as an
“ officer,” but recruited as a private and went
honestly through his drill until, he achieved the
grades of “full private,” “corporal,” &c. : arid
not all the unhallowed jeers of small souls
could move him from the dead routine
of rising by pure merit. This is the
man who by invitation addressed the N.S.
Wales Trades and Labour Council last week,
and had many kind words to giv.e unionism arid
struggling labour. He told them that they had
gained “increased respect with their increased
strength; ” he advised them to proceed warily
within legal lines; and explained to them the
principles of the measure he had drafted for the
8. A. Parliament, with a view to deal with strikes
and lockouts by a public tribunal. He encouraged
the Labour Electoral League to proceed with its
agitation for one-man-one-vote, and to work for
the Eight-hour Bill by instalments, first making
the Eight-hour system compulsory in all Govern-
ment departments and Government contracts, as
in South Australia. He is favourable to the
postal voting system for shearers, seamen and
other voters who are perforce absent from their
homes on election days ; to the abolition of money
deposits by candidates; and to a tax on unim-
proved land-values. With Sir S. W. Griffith, of
Queensland, and A. J. Clark, of Tasmania, C. 0.
Kingston drafted the rough skeleton of the Federal
Constitution Bill now before Convention.
The barbarous Chinese Judges torture an
accused person until he confesses his guilt {of an
alleged offence. r ihe barbarous Australian judicial
system coerces dissentient juries by locking them
up till they do agree. The governing principle is
the same—brute force; only the incidence and
degree of brutality differ.

A Midsummer Might’s
Idyll,
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It was a dejected-looking little town of the aeons,
tomed Queensland pattern, situate some 40 miles
or more up a hot muddy river that wound back
and forward, and back again, and round about;
as no river ever wound and serpentined before'
For eleven weary hours wo had been crawling in a
steamboat over the surface of this stream
even the shadow of it in the darkness became a
weariness to the eye, and as we went along I had
tramped monotonously about the deck, and won-
dered whether my legs- would not presently get
w#rn off right up to my stomach through sheer
exertion and disgust. There was nothing to be
heard except the gurgle and swish of the thick,
turbid water and the asthmatic snort of the steam*
pipe, and nothing to be seen except no ,v and then
the dusky image of a bald and naked stump on
the bank, and nothing to speculate about except
the chance of climbing safely over the next snag>
and the dim probability of crowding successfully
l ound the next corner, for the river was low, and,
as a consequence, it was even a shade more
angular than usual. Even a bird flying across it
would be likely to lose its way and find it-elf back
on the shore it started from, and whether there
was only one shore altogether, or whether there
were two, and both of them were the other one, I
never knew. Generally, we seemed to be plunging
Wl .th a strange sough and groan into the very
middle of a dense black shadow that rose up like
?! v jall in front of us, and then just as we touched
it the vessel would fly off at a tangent and make
burst for the bank, and when it got there
the bank would somehow prove to be absent, and
we would puff unconcernedly over a stretch of
dead black water until it was time to make anothc r
and bolt madly for a tree standing out on
the extreme end of a low headland. But the tree
would also melt away mysteriously, and the head-
tand would vanish, and after that all would go
well till we started to perform some more calis-
thenics, and make a fresh jump for destruction.
hen vve would steer uneasily round some more
corners, and the escape-pipe would blow forth a
Weolslirill derision, and next we would graze a
6 loaded with pumpkins, and the captain
would blaspheme from the deck and ask us, with
n anguished cry, where we supposed we were
to. No one took any notice of him, how-
,® r 5 the captain of a ketch doesn’t amount to
anything.
The steamer was old and slow, and gave forth
nornd and indescribable odour. The saloon
as small, and the cabins were stuffy. The bath-
use was on deck, but for some unexplained
ason when the crew started to wash the deck
- J r.V7u ter *' su PP l y ’ n the bath immediately gave out,
the crew washed the deck incessantly. I had
i on -°, chow-passenger, and she was a stout
.< 7 Wlt n a dog attached to a short string. Un-
arwi U ? ately ’ t r °d on the dog early in the voyage,
dric.fi m that moment the fount of sympathy
„ . U .P> ail d I found myself alone, and my only
aiumg amusement consisted in gazing at the
monotonous bank, and getting out of the path of
the cook when that artizan in boiled mutton fled
along the deck with a large dish in his hand. But
there was no thrill in either of these dissipations,
.and so a feeling of deep and holy joy filled my
soul when our vessel coughed wearily up to a
broken wharf, and I scrambled ashore.
There was a steep clay bank behind the wharf,
and on top of it the last earthly possibility in the
way of a hotel loomed out against the stars.
It seemed to have been budt mainly of old boards
and mud and disused gin-cases, but the materials
could not be very well defined in the darkne'ss—
there was merely a prevailing aspect of jaggedness
that belonged to no recognised order ot architec-
ture, and beyond it there was a hazy and unde-
fined street which appeared to have fallen down
dead while only half-grown. The street ran
parallel to the river, and consequently there was
mud at one side and real estate at the other—
the mud beingmuch the more numerous of the two.
As for the population, it had only one visible
representative—-a small, fat, old man, who stood
at the door of tire hotel with a candle in a
bottle, awaiting the course of events. He was a
hideously confidential old man, too, and when he
had conducted me into a bedroom with two win-
dows and one solitary chair and a stuffed oat in it,
he leaned up against the wall and conversed
affably, while I hung my hat on the bedpost and
listened, with one boot in my hand and my senses
buried in partial oblivion.
His daughter had eloped that morning, it ap-
peared, with a gentleman in the paving-stone and
road-repairing industry, and his wife had been
upset in her finer feelings and had gone to bed.
She had remained there all day, and it had been
found necessary to apply stimulants to keep her
up, and in consequence of this disaster lie had
been compelled to run the hotel himself, and wash
lip the dishes, and perform other menial duties.
For his own part, he had never looked with a
favourable eye on the gentleman who repaired
roads, and he was inclined to think that, as a
son-in-law, he would prove a failure. He had a
dull, far-away sound as he related these circum-
stances, and a thick haze seemed to gather round
him, and by-and-by his sentences appeared to fall
to pieces, and I fell asleep in a sitting position
and dreamed that his voice was a rushing river
somewhere —and then I awoke with a sudden
wrench which seemed to tear all my joints
asunder, and found him still talking. The candle
had burnt low in the bottle, and the grease was
guttering on the floor, and he had evidently got
to the end of the original subject, for he was
relating the incidents connected with a gold rush,
or a shearing riot, or something of the sort. I
suggested briefly that I was going to bed, and he
faded out of the door, and came back with a fresh
candle, and faded again, and came back once
more to supply some further information, and
then he faded away for the third time, and I
rushed at the door and locked it to keep him out.
After a while he returned and made a frantic
struggle to get into the room, but, failing in this,
he retired with a heavy sigh and went to bed.
The little town was unutterably still. There
was a ripple on the water, but it was merely the
impalpable ghost of an absent sound, and was
hardly more definite than the footsteps of the
moonlight as it began to steal feebly through the
window. But despite the stillness and my own
weariness I could only be still and curse inwardly,
and accumulate cramps. But by-and-bye I began
to drift into that undefinable condition when n
man is always doing something and never getting
to the bottom, or is filled with the tail-end of a
thousand brilliant ideas and loses them all the
next instant, and probably I might have dropped
off only the afflicted landlady suddenly went into
hysterics over her lost daughter and filled the
building with screams and disjointed observations.
Probably her husband emptied a water-jug over
her, for she dropped screaming and took to exe-
crating instead. She was evidently in the next
room, and it was also evident that she was a large
brawny female, for 1 heard her come out of bed
with a thud, and then there .came a series of
hollow reverberations as she plunged and gambolled
about on the floor. The next moment a window
opened, and a long white figure galloped along
the unsafe and treacherous-looking balcony which
fronted the building. Another figure, a short,
fat one, appeared in the moonlight a second
later and went by at a resolute trot carry-
ing its pants in one hand and a hat in the
other, and presently a wild-iookingobject also flew
by, throwing its limb? out in great, loose strides,
and uttering Irish ejaculations of surprise.
There was an excited argument at the other end
of the balcony, and the hysterical female seemed
to make several efforts to throw herself over into
the street, but at length she became pacified, and
■returned in a faint and limp condition—in other
words, her husband trailed her along in a series
of short jumps, and puffed a good deal under the
exertion. He wore his hat over one ear this time,
and carried his pants in his teeth. The third in-
dividual did not assist, but as the landlord jumped
his insensible prey back through the window that
fainting lady partially woke up and gave her fol-
lower notice to quit the house in the morning.
The place grew quiet again after tins—all
except a dog-'and-goat fight in the street below.
One of the combatants gave away most of
his ear in the course of this latter difficulty,
and the other had his tail wrecked. Finally, they
both departed in a cloud of dust, and escaped over
the horizon, and' left silence behind them, and I
resolved to go back to bed again, and fall asleep.
The bed was not favourable, however. It seemed
to be stuffed with bricks and coal, and it described
such an angle to the horizon that I had to brace
my feet against the lower .end of it in order to
keep my position. This is a hard world, and if
my feet slipped it was evident I would fall out on
to one of the hardest parts’of it. When I realised
this fact I sat up and made a remark—a long, in-
furiated remark which was unfit to print, and
while I was making it the bed-clothes slipped on
to the floor, and when I hauled them up again
they were tied in a hard knot, so that when I
tried to get into them I merely came out again at
the other end. Then I looked for the candle with
a view to putting things right once for all, and I
found it so suddenly that I filled my eye with
grease and wick, and in my anguish I prayed that
the eleven devils which howl for over round Judas
Iscariot might trample on the woman who made
the bed and on all her descendants till they be-
came extinct.
It was just at this moment that I noticed some-
one pounding ceaselessly at the front door, and
when I listened intently I became aware that
there was someone in tears on the footpath be-
low. I opened the window and implored her in
a loud voice to go home—to go and sleep on the
fence—to go to bed—to go to Gehenna if she pre-
ferred it, but, anyhow, to keep quiet and go, but
as she still wept and pounded I went out on the
balcony and considered the situation with my un-
greased eye.
There was a girl below with a carpet-bag and a
hat-box, and she was wailing bitterly and rush-
ing at the door with her feet. Sometimes she
jumped at it with both feet and at others she took
running kicks with only one, and then between
these exercises she would put her inflamed eye to
the keyhole and pray for admittance. Evidently
she was,in dire distress of some kind, and for a
while I was in doubt whether to he a father to
her or whether to look at the matter from a purely
selfish standpoint and empty the contents of the
water-jug over the balcony. While I was debat-
ing this point she sat down in the road and threat-
ened to grow hysterical, and then I suddenly gave
way to a sympathetic mood and addressed her.
“ Here,” 1 shouted, “for heaven’s sake stop
that infernal row, and go away.”
“ Let me in,” she responded.
“ I can’t,” I answered briefly, “ It isn’t my
hotel, and anyhow I’m in full-dress. Besides, I
don’t know where the door is, and I want to go
to sleep, and I wouldn’t let you in anyhow. What
on earth are you weeping in the street with a
carpet-bag for ? ”
Apparently this aggravated her, for she began
to bang the door with stones, and then I put my
head in at the window of the next room, and
roused the landlord, and informed him that an
insane girl was reducing his house to general
smash. He came out with a gun, just in time to
find his daughter breaking the bar-window with
road-metal. It was evidently an hysterical out-
burst on her part, for when he hailed her with
considerable strong language she broke down
miserably in the gutter— and explained.
She was the individual who eloped that morn-
ing, and she had come back to report that
marriage had been a failure. The bridegroom had
taken her home, and had straightway gone out
and got drunk, and was now sleeping in the ash-
barrel at the back-door, and consequently she had
packed her carpet-bag and her hat-box, and run
for the old public-house in a repentant mood. As
there was evidently nothing else to be done the
indignant parent opened the door and threw the
carpet-bag and the hat-box into the passage.
Also, he admonished his daughter severely on the
stairs, and, judging from sundry sounds that
reached me, that young lady went to bed in a
repentant mood.
Then I went to bed also, and lay down with the
clothes rolled up in a hard ball on my chest, and
fell asleep till the steamer at the wharf com-
menced to blow its whistle with a melancholy
cadence that broke oft’ into shrieks and variations
at intervals, and filled the town with a solemn
note of woe, and at the same moment a rooster
eight feet high began to offer a few remarks in the
back-yard, and a man went by shoving a barrow
with an ungreased wheel. I sat up in bed, and
consigned the rooster, and the wheelbarrow, and
the distracted mother in the next room, and the
broken-hearted daughter with the carpet-bag to
all the infernal gods, and then I began to smoke.
It was rather difficult work, owing to the extreme
steepness of the bed, and I had to hold my head
down with both feet in order to preserve my
equilibrium, but I got on pretty well for a while,
and then
Evidently the old woman was awake, and
mourning for her absent child, and evidently also
the stern male parent with the gun was consoling
her with the information that the joy and sun-
shine of their domestic hearth had rushed home
through the dust with her clothes in both hands,
and was now sleeping in a contrite mood in the
back-room. There was a smothered scream to
begin with, and I could hear the fond mother get

The year 1891 is the centenary of the
planting of the first grape-vine in Australia. Wine
has special privileges in this country. A man
may, at a small charge, get a license to sell Aus-
tralian wine only, but he cannot get a license to
sell Australian beer only. Wine is supplied at
once in almost any city restaurant, but beer has
to be sent out fqr to the nearest public-house.
If asked to drink, call for Wolfe s Schnapps.
Two young men have been arrested at
Candelo (N.S.W.) charged, with setting fire to the
rum-saturated clothing of an old man. That used
to be a favourite Riverina pastime. The writer
has seen flames, a foot high, ascending from a
glass or two of spirits that seethed in the hollow
on top of a boozy bushy’s greasy hat and a regular
prairie fire creeping up a squatter’s back from the
tails of his dress-suit till it scorched his back-hair*

commendation of Pears’ Soap has opened for it
a large sale in the United States. I am willing
to stand by every word in favour of it that I over
uttered. A man must be fastidious indeed who
is not satisfied with it.”
(Signed) HENRY WARD BEECHER.

out of bed with a “woosh !” and scuttle on two
ponderous naked feet down a passage, and into
the apartment on the other side of mine. Then I
recognised that a crisis was at hand, and I grasped
the floor with both hands, for it was certain that
this explosive ancestor would either weep the
roof off with tears of joy, or else would spank her
prodigal daughter there and then, and in either
case I expected the house to totter on its founda-
tions. As it happened, she did both, but it only
accelerated the row in a very small degree, and I
felt comparatively resigned—on the floor. There
was a reconciliation first of all, and the young
wife rested her tired head on her parent’s capaci-
ous bosom and forgot her woes—the bosom was
hollow, and the head seemed to be uncommonly
hard, and I could hear the concussion as they
came together; and when the first pathos of the
scene was over, the mother evidently remembered
her offspring’s shortcomings, and hit her on both
ears with a dishcloth or some similar weapon.
But presently they became reconciled again and
went to sleep in each other’s arms, and the old
prosaic father shored placidly in the other room,
and there was a great calm and oblivion that
lasted till long after daybreak. For a while I sat
and looked at the lights and shadows which the
moon described on the mountains beyond the
river, but after a while the mountains grew hazy
and indistinct, and the shadows commenced to
dance a fantastic waltz before my eyes, and next
I put the wrong end of my pipe in my mouth,
and came back to consciousness just as I had
swallowed half a pint of ashes. I coughed up
most of the consignment, and ate the rest, and
then I retired once more, with my head at the
lower end cf the bed and my feet up in the air,
and slept a dreamless sleep.
When I awoke at last someone was ringing a
breakfast-bell at the keyhole, and there was over
a quart of water in my upper ear. A tropical
thunderstorm was raging outside, and a cooling
stream of rain descended through the roof just
above my head, while another cataract came
down in a refreshing manner on my feet. Also
the room was nearly afloat, so I dressed hurriedly
in the passage. A wild, confused argument was
in progress downstairs, for the bridegroom of
yesterday had come along at an early hour to
demand his wife, and was vociferating in the bar.
The storm, it appeared, had aroused him from his
placid slumbers in the barrel at the back-door, and
he had crawled into bis home to find it abandoned
and desolate, and after that sundry inquiries in
various directions elicited the information that the
angel of his fireless hearth had been seen in a state
of distraction outside her father’s public-house in
the early hours of the morning. He demanded
her immediate return, but the young lady
remained in bed and sent down a message that for
the future they must look upon each other as
strangers, and the landlord sat on a keg in a
corner and leaned his fat and harassed head
against the wall, and the landlady stood with a
stony glare behind the bar, and professed to regard
the visitor as a perfect stranger who had dropped
in by accident in search of refreshment. This was
the situation at nine o’clock, bub at half-past nine
the bride came downstairs and threw herself into
her husband’s arms;’and at a quarter to ten
he was drinking affably along with his father-in-
law, and at ten o’clock the landlady cast them
both off in a tragic manner, and renounced them
for ever. Viewed by daylight I discovered that
the heroine was an exceedingly long girl of about
seventeen, with a flame-coloured head and a
nervous wink in the left eye.
When they were gone the bereaved mother pro-
ceeded, with an air of Roman fortitude, to count
the horn-handled two-pronged forks and other
portable articles. Whether they were all there
or not I never heard.
TITUS SALT.
“His Masterpiece.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
‘•Greenhide Billy” was a stockman on a
Clarence River cattle-station and admittedly the
biggest liar in the district. He had been for many
years pioneering in the Northern Territory, the
other side of the sun-down—a regular “ furthest-
out man ’’—and this assured his reputation among
station-hands who award rank according to amount
of experience. Young men who have always hung
around the home localities, doing a job of shearing
here or a turn at horsebreaking there, look with
reverence on the Riverine or Macquarie-River
shearers who come in with tales of runs where
they have 300,000 acres of freehold land and shear
250,000 sheep, and these again pale their ineffec-
tual fives before the glory of the Northern Territory
man who has all-comers on toast, because no one
can contradict him or check his figures, except
someone from the same locality. When two such
meet, however, they are not fools enough to cut
down quotations and spoil the market; no, they
mutually lie in support of each other, and make
all other bushmen feel mean and pitiful and inex-
perienced.
Sometimes a youngster would timidly ask
Greenhide Billy about the (to him) terra incognita :
“What soi’t of a place is it, Billy—how big are
the properties ? How many acres had you in the
place you were on ?”-
“Acres be d d !” Billy would scornfully
reply; “hear him talking about acres! D’ye
think we were blanked cockatoo selectors ! Out
there we reckon country by the hundred miles.
You orter say, ‘How many thousand miles of
country ?’ and then I’d understand you.”
Furthermore, according to Billy, they reckoned
the rainfall in the Territory by yards, not inches ;
he had seen blackfellows who could jump at least
three inches higher than anyone else had ever seen
a blaekfellow jump, and every bushman has seen
or personally known a blaekfellow who could jump
over six feet. Billy had seen bigger droughts,
better country, fatter cattle, faster horses, and
cleverer dogs, than any other man on the Clarence
River. But one night when the rain was on the
roof, and the river was rising with a moaning
sound, and the men were gathered round the fire
in the hut smoking and staring at the coals, Billy
turned himself loose and gave us his masterpiece.
* * *
“ I was drovin’ with cattle from Hungry -
banbone to old Corlett’s station on the Bucka-
towndown River (Billy always started his stories
with some paralysing bush names.) We had a
thousand head of store-cattle, wild mountain-bred
wretches, they’d charge you on sight, and they
were that handy with their horns they could
skewer a mosquito. There was one or two one-
eyed ca f tle among ’em, and you know how a one-
eyed beast always keeps movin’ away from the
mob, pokin’ away out to the edge of them so as
they won’t git on his blind side; and then by
stirrin’ about he keeps the others restless. They
had been scared once or twice and stamped, and
gave us all we could do to keep them together ;
and it was wet and dark and thundering, and it
looked like a real bad night for us. It was my
watch, and I was on one side of the cattle, like it
might be here, with a small bit of a fire ; and my
mate, Barcoo Jim, lie was right opposite on the
other side of the cattle, and he had gone to sleep
under a log. The rest of the men were in the
camp fast asleep. Every now and again I’d get
on my horse and prowl round the cattle quiet like,
and they seemed to be settled down all right, and
I was sitting by my fire holding my horse and
drowsing, when all of a sudden a blessed ’possum
ran out from some saplings and scratched up a
little tree right alongside me. I was half-asleep,
I suppose, and was startled ; anyhow, never
thinking what I was doing, I picked up afirestick
out of the fire and flung it at the ’possum. Whoop!
Before you could say ‘Jack Robertson’ that
thousand head of cattle were on their feet, and
they made one wild, headlong, mad rush right
over the place where poor old Barcoo Jim was
sleeping. There was no time to hunt up materials
for the inquest; I had tc keep those cattle
together, so I sprang into the saddle, dashed the
spurs into the old horse, dropped my head on his
mane, and sent him as hard as he could leg it
through the scrub to get the lead of the cattle
and steady them. It was brigalow, and you know
what that is. You know how the brigalow grows,”
continued Bill, “saplings about as thick as a
man’s arm, and that close together a dog can’t
open his mouth to bark in ’em. Well, those cattle
swept through that scrub levelling it like as if it
had been cleared for a railway line. They cleared
a track a quarter of a mile wide, and smashed
every stick, stump, and sapling on it. Yon could
hear them roaring and their hoofs thundering and
the,scrub smashing three or four miles off. And
where was I? I was racing parallel with the
cattle, with my head down on the horse’s neck,
letting him pick his way through the scrub in the
pitchy darkness. This went on for about four
miles, then the cattle began to get winded, and I
dug into the old stookhorse with the spurs, and
got in front, and then began to crack the whip
and sing out, so as to steady them a little ; after
awhile they dropped slower and slower, and I
kept the whip going. I got them all together in
a patch of open country, and there I rode round
and round ’em all night till dayligh* - . And how
I wasn’t killed in the scrub, goodness only knows;
for a man couldn’t ride in the daylight where I
did in the dark. The cattle were all knocked
about—horns smashed, legs broken, ribs torn ;
but they were all there, every solitary head of
’em ; and as soon as the daylight broke I took
’em back to the camp—that is, all that could
travel, because a few broken-legged ones I had to
leave.”
# * *
Billy paused in his narrative. He knew
that some suggestions would be made, by way of
compromise, to tone down the awful strength of the
yarn, and he prepared himself accordingly. His
motto was “No surrender,” he never abated one
jot of his statements, and if anyone chose to
remark on them, he made them warmer and
stronger, and absolutely flattened out the in-
truder.
“ That was a wonderful bit of ridin’ you done,
Billy,” said one of the men at last, admiringly
“ It’s a wonder you wasn’t killed. I’spose your
clothes was pretty well tore off your back with
the scrub?”
“ Never touched a twig,” said Billy.
“ Ah !” faltered the inquirer, “then no doubt
you had a real ringin’ good stock-horse that could
take you through a scrub like that full-split in the
dark, and not hit you against anything.”
“No, lie wasn’t a good ’un,” said Billy decisively,
“ he was the worst horse in the camp, and terrible
awkward in the scrub he was, always failin’ down
on his knees ; and his neck was so short you could
sit far back on him and pull his ears.”
Here that interrogator retired hurt ; he gave
Billy best. Another took up the running after a
pause.
“How did your mate get on, Billy? I s’pose
ho was trampled to a mummy ! ”
“No,” said Billy, “he wasn’t hurt a bit. I
told you he was sleeping under the shelter of a
little log. Well, when these cattle rushed they
swept over that log a thousand strong ; and every
beast of that herd took the log in his stride and
just missed landing on Barcbo Jimmy by about
four inches. We saw the tracks where they had
cleared him in the night—and fancy that, Fa
thousand head of cattle to charge over a man in
the dark and just miss him by a hair’s breadth, as
you might say !”
The men waited a while and smoked, to let this
statement soak well into their systems ; at last
ope rallied and had a final try to get a suggestion
in somewhere.
“It’s a wonder, then, Billy,” he said, “that
your mate didn’t come after you and give you a
hand to steady the cattle.”
“Well, perhaps it was,” said Billy, “only that
there was a bigger wonder than that at the back
of it.”
“What was that ?”
“My mate never woke all through it.”
Then the men knocked the ashes out of their
pipes and went to bed.

DIPSO.
Four soldiers stationed at Dum-Dum, a
suburb of Calcutta, on the hunt for liquor in the
dead of night, threw one Selim, whom they
dragged from his bed, into the tank and their
shot him as lie swam ashore. They were arrested
for murder, and the two who had not fired turned
Queen’s evidence, while several coloured men
swore positively to the ident ty of the other two.
Judge Norris directed the jury to acquit one man
named Bellow, but O’Hara was unanimously
found guilty and sentenced to death. The Leinster
Regiment thereupon threatened to revolt and
shoot every native they could find if their comrade
were hanged for killing a nigger, and so the case
was revised before a bench of five, when O’Hara
was acquitted. The natives are highly incensed,
and vow to get even.
Prince Napoleon, the “ Plon-PIon ” whose
death is cabled, was remarkable for at least three
achievements. Firstly, he shot a man dead some
20 years ago, was tried for the murder, and
acquitted on the ground that the other, one Victor
Noir, might also have meant shooting. Secondly,
he begat a son in whom the lineaments of Buona-
parte are so strikingly reproduced that he can, or
could, awhile back, be fixed up to look like the
ghost of the great Little Corporal. Thirdly, he
gave Napoleon III. the opportunity to say a
fairly smart thing. “You have nothing of the
First Napoleon about you,” complained Plon-
Plon, during one of their periodical rows. “Oh,
yes, I have: I have his relatives about me,”
responded Nap. the Third. The wheeze was
accepted as a fact when the Emperor spread it
abroad, and as both principals are now em-
balmed there is nobody left to swear that it wasn't
got off on the spur of the moment, but thought of
next day.
Some of the Premier Permanent Building
Society’s Balaclava (Vic.) properties were put up
at auction last week. For four villas, on each of
which the Society had lent £050, the highest bid
was £460 a-piece, the reserve being £500. Dow,
are you listening ?
Dr. George Ernest Morrison, who is
remembered in connection with New Guinea ex-
ploration of ’B3-’B4, has returned to the parental
roof at Geelong. Since he left Australia, about
six years back, he has graduated at Edinburgh
University, has held an important hospital ap-
pointment in Spain, and taken a Spanish diploma.
He has also wandered about in the States, the
West Indies and North Africa. He now yearns
.for another New Guinea boom. The most interest-
ing relic he has of that country is a native spear,
portions of which are still embedded in his manly
frame.
On the Melbourne Financial Exchange,
the question is, How does Goldbug stand ? Will
he still smirk around, and give his gorgeous
parties, while Mirams has gone to the gaol-wall,
and while there are even whispers about the
mighty Taylor, the man who peeled a £5O-note off
his chest when the plate came round ?
The unfortunate political scapegoat,
Reseigh Martin, journalist, sentenced to two
years’imprisonment for libelling Railway Com-
missioner Eddy, was recently removed from
Sydney to Maitland gaol, chained by wrist and
leg—by whose order/ oategoriod as a convict-
felon. Be it remembered that Martin was no
criminal against the community, but a mistaken
would-be reformer at worst, and that the brutality
now meted out to him seems envenomed with
political vindictiveness.
The Grand United Order of Vamoosing
Australian Bank-clerks, Unlimited, has, during
the last year, declared dividends and bonuses
amounting in all to over £188,000.
Mr. P. Lahitf, ‘a Wollongong man,- rn
sinking a prospecting shaft through hydraulic
limestone, since satisfactorily tested, came upon a
seam of coal. Water flooded him out, and he
applied to the N.S.W. Prospecting Board for
assistance to further prosecute his researches.
“In passing through limestone,”said the Board
in effect, “you have gone beyond the coal-mea-
sures, and therefore we can’t assist.” “But there
is the coal underneath the limestone,"was Lahiff’s
obstinate reply. “Oh, it can’t be; geology says
it can’t, and that settles it,” was the dogmatic
dictum of the Board. Lahitf applied to the
Minister for Mines, who “ didn’t feel just'fied,”
etc., evasively. Which is remindful of the en.
thusiasm with which the Department, some
months ago, assisted an influential syndi-
cate to look for coal a thousand or two
feet below the bed of Sydney harbour, and
would probably have been willing to subsidise
a proposal from the same syndicate to sink a shaft
in Uranus. The moral of all this is that geologists
have no final nor even safe knowledge of the
occurrence of minerals. Up North once, it
used to be geological gospel that gold couldn’t be
found in red granite formation, but the best gold
was found there through the accidental straying of
a digger’s “ old crock ” on to a red granite range.
Again, Mount Morgan ore was a knock-down con-
tradiction of old geological theories, and the same
surprises have frequently happened in connection
with'other mineral freaks in Nature's laboratory.
The N.S. Wales Prospecting Board has no call to
consider the occurrence of coal beneath limestone
a personal insult to itself.
“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal ,
Its the only paper which has a consistent and dis.
tindive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A. Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
Sy .den).
A United Australia and Protection again: I
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
'i lie Chinese.
Imperial Fed era t, i on.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers o:
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos.
sessos a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive‘paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It bad at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every' Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable faot—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is tlie one most frequently reinailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian ‘
public.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To H. P. Kuoblmann, Esq.
Dear Sir, —This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
J became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up Wood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN,
Declared and subscribed before mo, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Pbtbr Smyths, J.P.
